Judge's Inquiry into Witnesses: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the judge's duty to inquire about witnesses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on the judge's inquiry into witnesses' integrity and apply the mufta bihi ruling in a given scenario.

Core Idea
The judge must inquire about witnesses' integrity in all cases, due to prevalence of corruption (Sahibayn's view mufta bihi).

The question of whether a judge must actively investigate the integrity of witnesses arises from a tension between the presumption of Muslim uprightness and the need for reliable proof. In this passage, the commentator explains the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, providing the evidence for each side and noting the shift in fatwa over time.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Read the passage to find the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Sahibayn). Note that the base text presents Abu Hanifa's view, and then the commentary adds 'Qala: ... wa lahum ...'.
Pay attention to the pronoun 'Qala' referring to the author of the commentary and 'lahuma' for the two companions.
2
Understand Abu Hanifa's proof
Examine the hadith narrated by Ibn Abi Shaybah: 'Muslims are trustworthy witnesses for each other, except one flogged for slander.' Also note the rational argument that apparent integrity suffices due to intellectual and religious deterrence.
The hadith is a hadith ahad (zanni al-thubut).
3
Analyze the exception for hadd and qisas
Why does Abu Hanifa make an exception for hadd and qisas? Because these penalties are warded off by doubts (shubhat), so extra investigation is needed to avoid unjust punishment.
This exception applies even without an opponent's objection.
4
Understand the Sahibayn's proof
The Sahibayn argue that judgment is based on proof (shahadat al-'udul), and apparent integrity (zahir al-'adalah) is insufficient proof. Therefore, the judge must investigate to ensure validity.
They reject the sufficiency of the general presumption in all cases.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its rationale
The text states that fatwa is given on the Sahibayn's view due to the prevalence of corruption in later times. Also, note the nuance that if the judge already knows the witness's character, no inquiry is needed.
This is a difference of era, not a contradiction in principles.

Except in the case of hadd and qisas, and the two companions said: He asks in all cases. [Commentary:] His saying: 'He does not ask... etc.' So the judge restricts himself to the apparent integrity of a Muslim, and does not ask about him until the opponent objects, except in hadd and qisas. Because Ibn Abi Shaybah narrated in his Musannaf from 'Amr ibn Shu'ayb from his father from his grandfather that the Messenger of Allah said: 'Muslims are trustworthy witnesses for each other, except one who has been flogged for slander.' And because the apparent state is that he is deterred (from sin), for his intellect and religion prevent him from committing evil. So one is satisfied with the apparent state when there is no opponent. Unlike hadd and qisas, because they are warded off by doubts, and caution is used to drop them in each instance from the start without the opponent's objection, hoping that they may be dropped. His saying: 'When the opponent does not object' – But if he objects, then the judge asks, for the opponent does not object falsely as a matter of appearance; so two apparent states conflict, and preference is required through thorough investigation. His saying: 'And the two companions said: He asks in all cases' – i.e., in all rights and claims, whether the opponent objects or not. Because judgment is based on proof, and that is the testimony of trustworthy witnesses. Integrity before inquiry is established by appearance, but that is not sufficient as proof for entitlement. So inquiry about them is necessary to safeguard against voidness. And according to their view fatwa is given. Thus in al-Hidayah and others, due to the prevalence of corruption in judgment in this time. And it is said: This is a difference of era and time. For Abu Hanifah was in the second century, and the Messenger of Allah testified to the goodness and righteousness of his people, while his two companions came after him, and people's conditions changed, and betrayals and lies increased. So they said. And the place of inquiry according to their view is when the judge is ignorant of their state. Similarly in al-Bahr, citing from al-Multaqat: 'When the judge knows the witnesses to be discredited or reliable, he does not ask about them.' And fatwa is given on this in our time, and it is sufficient to do it secretly and openly.
Source pages p. 532

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the judge's duty to inquire into the integrity (adalah) of witnesses. The base text (matn) states Abu Hanifa's position: the judge does not ask unless the opponent objects, except in hadd and qisas cases where inquiry is automatic. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that the judge must ask in all cases, regardless of objection. The commentary first provides Abu Hanifa's proof: a hadith that Muslims are presumed trustworthy except those punished for slander, and a rational argument that a Muslim's intellect and faith deter him from evil, so the judge can rely on apparent integrity in the absence of an opponent. However, in hadd and qisas, extra caution is needed because these are warded off by doubts. Then the commentary explains that if the opponent objects, the judge must investigate because the objection itself creates a conflicting appearance. Then it explains the Sahibayn's view: judgment is based on proof, and apparent integrity is insufficient proof; therefore inquiry is necessary. The fatwa is given on their view due to widespread corruption. The commentary also notes that this difference is due to changing times: Abu Hanifa lived in a more upright era, while later times saw more dishonesty. Therefore, in our time, the judge should inquire even if he knows the witnesses, unless he already knows their character. Finally, the passage concludes that secret tazkiyah suffices (which is explained in the next lesson).

Key Terms
العدالة (al-'adalah): The inner integrity and outward uprightness of a witness that makes his testimony acceptable.
الطعن (al-ta'n): The formal objection raised by the opponent against a witness's integrity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school, what is the judge's duty regarding inquiring into witnesses' integrity?
A) He must inquire only when the opponent objects.
B) He must inquire in all cases, whether the opponent objects or not.
C) He must inquire only in hadd and qisas cases.
D) He never inquires; he relies on apparent integrity.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi requires judges to proactively inquire about witnesses' integrity in all cases, reflecting a shift from the earlier presumption of uprightness due to changing conditions.

Secret vs. Public Tazkiyah of Witnesses
Explaining the preferred method of tazkiyah: secretly to avoid strife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the preferred method of tazkiyah in later times and describe the procedures for both secret and public tazkiyah.

Core Idea
Secret tazkiyah is preferred to avoid fitna, though both methods are valid.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the preferred method for verifying witnesses' integrity (tazkiyah). While public tazkiyah was common in early Islam, later scholars, including Imam Muhammad, warned that public disclosure of faults leads to enmity. Hence, secret tazkiyah became the operative practice to protect social harmony and ensure honest testimony. The text details how each form is conducted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two methods
Recognize that tazkiyah can be done secretly (sirran) or openly ('alanan). The passage explains both forms.
2
Understand the preference for secrecy
Identify the reason: public tazkiyah is described as 'affliction and tribulation' because it stirs enmity and may prevent honest verification.
The same statement is attributed to Imam Muhammad.
3
Learn the form of secret tazkiyah
The judge sends a sealed document (ruq'ah masturah) containing the witness's details to a verifier, who writes back whether the witness is trustworthy, unknown, or known for immorality.
If known for immorality, he remains silent or writes 'Allah knows best' unless silence would lead to injustice.
4
Learn the form of public tazkiyah
The verifier and witness are brought together, and the verifier is asked to declare his opinion openly.
This was the practice in early times.
5
Note the historical shift
Early practice favored public tazkiyah, but later jurists (including in al-Hidayah) deemed secret tazkiyah sufficient and preferred to avoid fitna.
The fatwa today follows the secret method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنَّه قد قيل : تزكية العلانية بلاء وفتنة، فإنّ المزكِّي إن أعلن مساوئ الشَّاهِدِ يُهيج بينهما عداوة وبغضاء ، وربما يمنعه الخوف أو الحياء أو غيرهما عن أن يقول في الشَّاهد ما هو حق. قوله : سراً ؛ وصورتُهُ : أن يبعث رقعةً ويقال لها : المستورة سترها عن أعين الناس، وفيها أسماء الشهود إلى المزكي، ويكتب في ذلك البياض نسب الشاهد، وحليته ومسجده الذي يصلي فيه ، ثم يكتب المزكي الذي بعث القاضي إليه عدالته بأن يكتب هو عدل جائز الشهادة ، وإن لم يعرفه لشيء كتب: هو مستور. ومن عرفه بالفسق لم يصرح به بل يسكت تحرزاً عن هتك الستر، أو يكتب : الله تعالى أعلم به ، إلا إذا عدله غيره، وخافَ أنه إن لم يصرّح به يقضى بشهادته يصرح به. كذا في «البناية» وغيرها. قوله : علناً ؛ قال في «المصباح المنير» : عَلَنَ الأمْرُ عُلُوناً، من باب قعد : ظَهَرَ وَانْتَشَرَ، فهو عَالِنٌ، وَعَلِنَ عَلَناً من باب تَعِبَ لغةٌ : فهو عَلِنٌ وَعَلين، والاسم العلانية مخفف. انتهى. وصورته : أن يجمع بين المزكّي وبين الشاهد الذي زكاه ، ويقول للمزكّي الذي زكيته. ذكره الحموي. قوله : ويكفي ؛ وقد كانت العلانية قصدها في الصدر الأول، ووقع الاكتفاء في السِّرِّ في زماننا ؛ تحرّزاً من الفتنة ، ويروى عن محمد الله تزكية العلانية بلاء وفتنة. كذا في «الهداية». قوله : بمساوئ ؛ المَساءةُ : نقيضُ المَسَرَّةِ وأصلُها مَسْوَأَةٌ على مَفْعَلَةٍ بفتح الميم والعين؛ ولهذا تُرَدُّ الواو في الجمع فيقال : هي المساوي لكن استعمل الجمع مخففا وبدت مساويه أي نقائصه ومعايبه. كذا في «المصباح».
For it was said: Public tazkiyah is affliction and tribulation. For the verifier, if he discloses the faults of the witness, it stirs up enmity and hatred between them, and perhaps fear, shame, or other things prevent him from saying what is true about the witness. His saying: 'secretly' – Its form is that he sends a sealed note, called the 'covered one' (al-mastura), hidden from the eyes of people. In it are the names of the witnesses sent to the verifier. In that blank space, he writes the lineage of the witness, his description, and the mosque in which he prays. Then the verifier to whom the judge sent writes his integrity: he writes 'He is trustworthy, acceptable testimony.' If he does not know him for anything, he writes: 'He is maqbuul (acceptable).' If he knows him for immorality, he does not declare it openly but remains silent to avoid violating his cover, or he writes: 'Allah knows him best.' Except if someone else has declared him trustworthy and he fears that if he does not declare it, judgment will be given based on his testimony, then he declares it. Thus in al-Binayah and others. His saying: 'openly' – He said in al-Misbah al-Munir: 'Alana al-amr 'ulunan, from the verb qa'ada: it appeared and spread, so it is 'alin. And 'alina 'alanan from the verb ta'iba is a language: so it is 'alin and 'aliyyun, and the noun al-'alaniyyah is lightened.' End. Its form: that the verifier is brought together with the witness he verified, and he says to the verifier: 'The one you verified.' Mentioned by al-Hamawi. His saying: 'and it suffices' – In the early period, public tazkiyah was intended, but in our time, they have been satisfied with secret tazkiyah to avoid tribulation. And it is narrated from Muhammad that public tazkiyah is affliction and tribulation. Thus in al-Hidayah. His saying: 'faults' – al-masa'a is the opposite of al-masarra (joy), its origin is maswa'ah on the pattern of maf'alah with fatha of the mim and 'ayn. Hence the waw is restored in the plural, so it is said: al-masawi, but the plural is used lightly, and 'badat masawihi' means his faults and blemishes. Thus in al-Misbah.
Source pages p. 533

Commentary
This passage discusses the methodology of tazkiyah (verifying witnesses' integrity). It begins by stating that public tazkiyah (al-'alaniyah) is a trial and tribulation because it creates enmity and may discourage the verifier from being honest. The preferred method is secret tazkiyah (sirran). The form of secret tazkiyah: the judge sends a sealed document (ruq'ah mastura) containing the witnesses' names, lineage, description, and mosque to a trusted verifier. The verifier writes back: if the witness is known to be trustworthy, he writes 'he is just and acceptable'; if unknown, 'he is maqbuul' (acceptable); if known to be immoral, he either remains silent to avoid exposing him, or writes 'Allah knows him best', unless someone else has declared him trustworthy and failure to speak would lead to an unjust judgment, in which case he must declare. Public tazkiyah: the verifier is brought before the witness and asked to declare his opinion. The commentary then notes that while public tazkiyah was practiced in the early period, secret tazkiyah is now sufficient and preferred to avoid fitna. It also explains the word 'masawi' (faults).

Key Terms
التزكية (al-tazkiyah): The process of verifying the integrity ('adalah) of a witness by a qualified person.
الرقعة المستورة (al-ruq'ah al-masturah): A sealed document sent by the judge to the verifier, containing the names and details of witnesses, concealed from public view.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Al-'alaniyah
B) Al-ruq'ah al-masturah
C) Al-masawi
D) Al-muzakki

Key Takeaway
Secret tazkiyah is the preferred method in later times to avoid fitna, but both secret and public are valid.

Secret vs. Open Tazkiyah in Hanafi Law
Ruling on tazkiyah with proof and procedure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on secret vs. open tazkiyah and explain why secrecy is preferred in contemporary practice.

Core Idea
Secret tazkiyah suffices today to avoid fitna.

In the Hanafi school, the method of certifying a witness's integrity (tazkiyah) has evolved. While open tazkiyah was practiced in early Islam, later jurists recognized that it could cause enmity and discourage truthful testimony. This lesson examines the proof for preferring secrecy and the practical procedure for its implementation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The text states that secret tazkiyah suffices and is the basis for fatwa in our time. Open tazkiyah is not invalid but is discouraged to avoid fitna.
The ruling is sunnah (recommended) in the sense of being the preferred practice.
2
Understand the proof (dalil)
The proof is a narration from Imam Muhammad: 'Open tazkiyah is tribulation and fitna.' The term 'fitna' indicates harm, so secrecy avoids that harm.
The dalil is zanni in both transmission and meaning, but it supports istihsan.
3
Apply the procedure for secret tazkiyah
The judge sends a concealed note (al-masturah) to the certifier, who writes the witness's integrity or states 'unknown' or remains silent if faults are known, unless disclosure is necessary.
This procedure protects both parties and maintains social harmony.
4
Note the shift from early practice
Open tazkiyah was common in the early period (sadr al-awwal), but later scholars relied on secrecy to prevent fitna.
This shows that rulings can adapt to changing circumstances while preserving the objective of justice.

تاب الشهادة والرجوع عنها تعريفها ونصابها وما يتحمله الشاهد. سراً وعلناً، وبه يُفْتَى في زماننا، ويكفي سراً، فإنَّه قد قيل: تزكية العلانية بلاء وفتنة، فإنّ المزكِّي إن أعلن مساوئ الشَّاهِدِ يُهيج بينهما عداوة وبغضاء، وربما يمنعه الخوف أو الحياء أو غيرهما عن أن يقول في الشَّاهد ما هو حق. قوله: سراً؛ وصورتُهُ: أن يبعث رقعةً ويقال لها: المستورة سترها عن أعين الناس، وفيها أسماء الشهود إلى المزكي، ويكتب في ذلك البياض نسب الشاهد، وحليته ومسجده الذي يصلي فيه، ثم يكتب المزكي الذي بعث القاضي إليه عدالته بأن يكتب هو عدل جائز الشهادة، وإن لم يعرفه لشيء كتب: هو مستور. ومن عرفه بالفسق لم يصرح به بل يسكت تحرزاً عن هتك الستر، أو يكتب: الله تعالى أعلم به، إلا إذا عدله غيره، وخافَ أنه إن لم يصرّح به يقضى بشهادته يصرح به. كذا في «البناية»، وغيرها، وإن شئت التفصيل فارجع إلى حاشية شيخنا العلامة الطحطاوي على «الدر المختار». قوله: علناً؛ قال في «المصباح المنير»: عَلَنَ الأمْرُ عُلُوناً، من باب قعد: ظَهَرَ وانْتَشَرَ، فهو عَالِنٌ، وعَلِنَ عَلَناً من باب تَعِبَ لغةٌ: فهو عَلِنٌ وعَلين، والاسم العلانية مخفف. انتهى. وصورته: أن يجمع بين المزكّي وبين الشاهد الذي زكاه، ويقول للمزكّي الذي زكيته. ذكره الحموي. قوله: ويكفي؛ وقد كانت العلانية مقصودة في الصدر الأول، ووقع الاكتفاء في السِّرِّ في زماننا؛ تحرّزاً من الفتنة، ويروى عن محمد رحمه الله تزكية العلانية بلاء وفتنة. كذا في «الهداية». قوله: بمساوئ؛ المَساءةُ: نقيضُ المَسَرَّةِ وأصلُها مَسْوَأَةٌ على مَفْعَلَةٍ بفتح الميم والعين؛ ولهذا تُرَدُّ الواو في الجمع فيقال: هي المساوي لكن استعمل الجمع مخففا وبدت مساويه أي نقائصه ومعايبه. كذا في «المصباح».
Book of Testimony and Withdrawal from It: Definition, Quorum, and What the Witness Bears. Secretly and openly, and it is issued as a fatwa in our time, and it suffices secretly, for it has been said: Open tazkiyah is tribulation and fitna, for if the one performing tazkiyah discloses the faults of the witness, it incites enmity and hatred between them, and perhaps fear, shyness, or other things prevent him from saying what is true about the witness. His statement: 'Secretly'; its form: that he sends a note, called al-masturah, concealed from people's eyes, containing the names of the witnesses to the one performing tazkiyah, and in that blank space he writes the lineage of the witness, his description, and the mosque where he prays. Then the one performing tazkiyah, to whom the judge sent, writes his integrity by writing 'He is just, admissible in testimony,' and if he does not know him for anything, he writes 'He is one whose condition is unknown.' And if he knows him for immorality, he does not disclose it explicitly but remains silent to avoid tearing the veil, or writes 'Allah the Most High knows best about him,' unless someone else has vouched for him and he fears that if he does not disclose it, judgment will be passed based on his testimony, then he discloses it. This is as in al-Binayah and others, and if you want details, refer to the gloss of our teacher al-Allamah al-Tahtawi on al-Durr al-Mukhtar. His statement: 'Openly'; it is said in al-Misbah al-Munir: 'Alana al-amru' 'ulunan, from the paradigm of qa'ada: it appeared and spread, so it is 'alin, and 'alina 'alanan from the paradigm of ta'iba is a language: so it is 'alin and 'alin, and the noun is al-'alaniyah, lightened. End. Its form: that he gathers between the one performing tazkiyah and the witness whom he vouched for, and says to the one performing tazkiyah: 'Whom you vouched for.' Mentioned by al-Hamawi. His statement: 'And it suffices'; open tazkiyah was intended in the early period, and reliance on secrecy has occurred in our time to avoid fitna, and it is reported from Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him): 'Open tazkiyah is tribulation and fitna.' This is as in al-Hidayah. His statement: 'With faults'; al-masa'ah is the opposite of al-masarrah, and its origin is maswa'ah on the pattern of maf'alah with fatha on both the mim and 'ayn; for this reason, the waw is restored in the plural, so it is said: al-masawi, but the plural is used lightened, and 'badat masawih' meaning his shortcomings and defects. This is as in al-Misbah.
Source pages p. 533

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary on Kitab al-Shahadah (Book of Testimony) and discusses the method of tazkiyah (character certification). The author states that in earlier times, tazkiyah was performed openly, but in the current era, the fatwa is that secret tazkiyah suffices, and this is the relied-upon ruling. The reason is that open tazkiyah can lead to fitna (tribulation) and enmity between the witness and the one certifying. If the certifier openly mentions the witness's faults, it may cause hatred, and also may prevent the certifier from speaking the truth due to fear or shyness. The author then details the procedure for secret tazkiyah: the judge sends a concealed note, called al-masturah, containing the witness's identity to the certifier, who then writes 'he is just, admissible in testimony' if known for integrity, or 'unknown' if not known. If the witness is known for immorality, the certifier remains silent or writes 'Allah knows best' to avoid exposing him, unless another has vouched for him and silence would lead to injustice. The passage also defines linguistic terms: 'alaniyah' is derived from 'alana' meaning to become public, and 'masawi' is the plural of 'maswa'ah' meaning faults. The overall ruling is that secret tazkiyah is sufficient in our time, and this is the position of the Hanafi school as mentioned in al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
تزكية (tazkiyah): The process of certifying a witness's integrity ('adalah) for the court, usually by a trustworthy person or by the judge's inquiry.
سراً (sirran): Secretly: the method of tazkiyah where the judge sends a sealed note to the certifier, avoiding public disclosure.
علناً (alaniyatan): Openly: the method where the certifier publicly declares the witness's integrity, often in court.
مستورة (masturah): The sealed note sent by the judge to the certifier in secret tazkiyah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because open tazkiyah is invalid
B) To avoid fitna and enmity between the witness and the certifier
C) Because witnesses are not trustworthy
D) Because the judge cannot be present

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prefers secret tazkiyah to prevent fitna, and this is the operative ruling today.

Number of Witnesses for Tazkiyah: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling that one witness suffices for tazkiyah and the ikhtilaf regarding number

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the number of witnesses required for tazkiyah, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute, and identify the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
One witness suffices for tazkiyah because it is a religious matter, not a legal testimony.

Tazkiyah is the process of certifying a witness's integrity before the court. This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute: does tazkiyah require one witness or two? The answer affects how judges verify witness credibility and reveals deeper usul principles about analogical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi Position
Identify the position of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: one just witness (or the judge's tazkiyah of the witness himself) suffices for tazkiyah.
The text says 'wa kafa wahid' (one suffices) and 'al-asahh' (most sound) is that view.
2
Identify the Opposing View
Recognize that Muhammad al-Shaybani requires two witnesses for tazkiyah, and this is also the view of the three other schools.
Muhammad's position is transmitted in the nawadir and is mentioned as 'al-ithna ahwat' (two is more precautionary).
3
Understand the Proof of the Opponent
Analyze the analogy (qiyas) used: tazkiyah is like shahadah (testimony) because both establish integrity for judicial purposes, so the condition of two witnesses should apply.
This analogy is a qiyas khafi (hidden analogy) based on the illa of establishing 'adalah.
4
Understand the Hanafi Response
See how Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf refute the analogy: tazkiyah is a religious matter (min umur al-din), not a legal claim, so only integrity ('adala) is required, not a specific number.
They also note that a slave, woman, or even a repentant qadhif can perform tazkiyah in most cases, supporting the view that number is not required.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi
Conclude that the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is one witness suffices, as indicated by 'al-asahh' in the text.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that Hanafi judges accept one, while others require two.

This is as in al-Minah. His statement: 'And one suffices...' etc.; meaning that one person is suitable to be a tazkiyah provider for the witness, a translator for the witness, and a messenger from the judge to the tazkiyah provider according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf); because tazkiyah is from religious matters, so only integrity is required in it, such that tazkiyah from a slave, a woman, a blind person, and one who was flogged for false accusation [of fornication] if he repents is permissible; because their reports are accepted in religious matters, and two is better because it increases confidence. And according to Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him), two are necessary, and it is the saying of the three imams; because tazkiyah is in the meaning of shahadah; because the authority of the judiciary is based on the manifestation of integrity, and it is through tazkiyah, so number is required in it just as integrity is required, and masculinity is required in the tazkiyah provider for hudud and qisas.
Source pages p. 534

Commentary
This passage from the sharh focuses on two key rulings regarding tazkiyah: (1) the sufficiency of one witness for tazkiyah, and (2) the invalidity of the opponent (khasm) as a certifier. The author, commenting on the matn, explains that to certify a witness, saying 'huwa adl' (he is just) is sufficient in the most sound view, because freedom and Islam are presumed from residence in an Islamic land. The opponent's certification is invalid because the opponent is biased and unlikely to be objective. The passage then presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (al-Shaykhan) hold that one witness suffices for tazkiyah, as tazkiyah is a religious matter and only integrity is required. Muhammad al-Shaybani, however, requires two witnesses, analogizing tazkiyah to shahadah (testimony) where two male witnesses are generally needed. The text notes that two are more precautionary. The dispute has practical implications: in courts following the Hanafi school, the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that of the Shaykhan, so one just witness is enough. The passage also notes that a slave, woman, blind person, or even a repentant person flogged for qadhf can perform tazkiyah in non-hudud matters, but for hudud and qisas, the certifier must be male.

Key Terms
تزكية (tazkiyah): Certification of a witness's integrity; the process of establishing 'adalah before the court.
عدل (adl): A just person; here, the statement 'huwa adl' is sufficient for tazkiyah.
خصم (khasm): The opponent in a lawsuit; the text rules that an opponent cannot validly perform tazkiyah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Four witnesses are required for tazkiyah

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, one witness suffices for tazkiyah because it is a religious matter, not a legal testimony; the opponent cannot perform tazkiyah due to bias.

Number of Tazkiyah Witnesses: One or Two?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on number of tazkiyah witnesses and conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the number of muzzaki required for secret tazkiyah and explain the reasoning behind the majority view.

Core Idea
One muzzaki suffices for secret tazkiyah; the number requirement from testimony does not extend because tazkiyah is not testimony.

This lesson examines a key intra-Hanafi dispute: how many people are needed to validate a witness's character (tazkiyah)? While Imam Muhammad required two, the majority of the school—Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf—held that one is enough for secret tazkiyah. Understanding the reasoning clarifies the nature of tazkiyah and its distinction from court testimony.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its rulings
Tazkiyah is the process of verifying a witness's integrity. Two scenarios exist: secret tazkiyah (done privately) and open tazkiyah (public testimony). Note the ruling for each.
Open tazkiyah requires two muzzaki by consensus.
2
State the Hanafi majority position
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, one muzzaki suffices for secret tazkiyah. This is the mufta bihi (operative) view.
3
State the opposing view
Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani requires two muzzaki for secret tazkiyah, equating it with testimony.
4
Analyze the proof
The majority argues that tazkiyah is not testimony because it lacks formal court language and setting. The requirement of number in testimony is by text (nass), not by analogy, so it does not extend to tazkiyah.
The passage mentions the analogy with hadith transmission where one trustworthy narrator suffices.
5
Determine the practical consequence
If the opponent consents to one muzzaki, it is valid by consensus. If the opponent objects, the dispute becomes operative, and the judge may require two for caution.
The point of ikhtilaf is only when the opponent does not consent.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكفى واحد للتزكية ( وكفى للتزكية : هو عدل في الأصح، فإنَّهُ قد قيل : لا بُدَّ أن يقول : هو عدل جائزُ الشَّهادة، لكنَّ الأصح هو الأَوَّل ؛ لأنَّ الحرية تثبت بدار الإسلام فإذا قال : هو عدل ، يكون جائزُ الشَّهادة ، ولا يصح تعديل الخصم ؛ بقوله : هو عدل أخطأ أو نسي، فإن قال : عَدْلٌ صَدَق ، ويَثْبُتُ الحق). وترجمة الشاهد والرسالة إلى المزكي، والاثنان أحوط، هذا عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف، وأمَّا عند محمَّد يجب الاثنان، وهذا في تزكيةِ السِّرِّ، أما في تزكية العلانية ، فقد قال الخَصَّافُ : يَحِبُ الاثنان إجماعاً ؛ لأنها في معنى الشهادة، حتّى لا يصح تزكية العلانية من العبد، ولا بد أن يكون المزكي عدلاً، فلا تُقبل تزكية الفاسق ومستور الحال. والجواب: إنها ليست في معنى الشهادة ؛ ولهذا لا يشترط فيها لفظ الشهادة ومجلس القضاء، واشتراط العددِ في الشهادة أمرٌ ثابت بالنص، بخلاف القياس ؛ لأنّ رجحان الصدق في العمل بالعدالة لا بالعدد، كما في رواية حديث النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في حق العلم بالتواتر. وإذا كان كذلك لا معنى لاشتراط العدد في الشهادات ، لكنا تركنا ذلك القياس بالنصوص التي فيها بيان العدد، فلا يتعدّى اشتراط العددِ من الشهادات إلى التزكية ، قال في «مجمع الأنهر) : ومحل الاختلاف ، فإذا لم يرض الخصم بتزكية الواحد، فإن رضي فجاز إجماعاً. انتهى.
And one is sufficient for tazkiyah: 'He is just' is the most correct, and the modification of the opponent is not valid by his saying 'He is just' if he erred or forgot. But if he said 'just', it is true and the right is established. And one is sufficient for tazkiyah: He is just is the most correct, for it has been said: He must say 'He is just, permissible of testimony', but the most correct is the first because freedom is established by the abode of Islam, so if he says 'He is just', he is permissible of testimony. The answer: It is not like testimony; for this reason, the wording of testimony and the court session are not required. The requirement of number in testimony is established by text, unlike analogy, because the preponderance of truthfulness in action is by justice, not number, as in the narration of the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) regarding knowledge through tawatur. And if that is so, there is no meaning for requiring number in testimonies, but we left that analogy due to texts that specify number, so the requirement of number is not extended from testimonies to tazkiyah. Source pages p. 534

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the number of tazkiyah witnesses required to validate a witness in court. The commentator first clarifies that the correct statement for tazkiyah is simply 'he is just', not necessarily 'he is permissible of testimony', because freedom is assumed for those raised in Dar al-Islam. Then the main issue: According to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf, one muzzaki (person performing tazkiyah) is sufficient. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, however, requires two. This applies to secret tazkiyah (done without public announcement). For open tazkiyah (public testimony about the witness's character), al-Khassaf reports consensus that two are needed, as it is akin to testimony itself. The commentator then gives the Hanafi response: tazkiyah is not like testimony because it does not require the formal wording of testimony or the court session. The requirement of number in testimony is based on explicit texts (e.g., Quran 2:282), not on the underlying reason (justice). Since the rationale for number is not the same, it does not extend to tazkiyah. The passage ends with a quote from Majma' al-Anhur: the difference is only when the opponent does not consent; if the opponent agrees to one muzzaki, it is permissible by consensus.

Key Terms
تزكية (tazkiyah): The process of verifying a witness's integrity and competence; validation performed by a muzzaki.
مزكي (muzzaki): The person who performs tazkiyah, i.e., attests to the witness's justice.
العدالة (al-'adalah): The requisite justice/integrity that makes a person's testimony or tazkiyah acceptable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) One is sufficient.
B) Two are required.
C) At least three.
D) None, the witness's own word is enough.

Key Takeaway
For secret tazkiyah, one muzzaki suffices according to the Hanafi majority; two is more cautious.

Testifying Without Being Asked: Audible and Visible Matters
Explaining the first type of testimony a witness can bear without being asked

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the first category of testimony a witness can bear without being asked and apply the condition of certainty of identity when hearing from behind a screen.

Core Idea
Directly heard or seen matters can be testified without formal request; but voice similarity blocks testimony unless certainty is achieved.

In this passage, the commentator expands on the first type of testimony: what is 'established by itself.' This includes sales, confessions, rulings, usurpation, and killings — things the witness directly heard or saw. The key nuance is that hearing from behind a screen introduces doubt due to possible voice resemblance, so testimony is only allowed when certainty of identity is possible, such as when the house has only one occupant with no other exit. This reflects the Hanafi emphasis on certainty in testimony (shahadah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of testimony bearing
The author divides what a witness can bear into two: (1) what is established by itself without being asked, and (2) what requires being asked. This lesson focuses on the first.
The first category covers acts the witness directly heard or saw.
2
List the examples given for audible and visible matters
Audible: sale (hearing 'I sold' and 'I bought'), confession, judge's ruling. Visible: usurpation, killing. These are 'thabit al-hukm binafsihi' (established in ruling by themselves).
The ruling is established by the event itself, so no request is needed.
3
Understand the exception: hearing from behind a screen
If the witness hears from behind a screen, testimony is not permissible because one voice can resemble another. Except when certainty is attained (e.g., only one man in the house with no other path).
The principle 'al-naghamatu tushbihu al-naghamah' creates doubt that prevents certainty.
4
Apply the condition of certainty for a woman's voice
Hearing a woman's voice from behind a screen is insufficient; the witness must see her person to testify. This is from al-Zayla'i and other jurists.
Same principle: voice resemblance without visual identification is not enough.
5
Determine the judge's role in accepting such testimony
If the witness explains the circumstances (e.g., the house had only one occupant), the judge may accept it because certainty is achieved. But the permissibility to testify does not force acceptance.
The judge evaluates the degree of certainty.

Source Text

ولمن سمع بيعاً، أو إقراراً، أو حكم قاض، أو رأى غصباً، أو قتلاً أن يشهد به، وإن لم يُشَهد عليه

فصل في بيان أنواع ما يتحمله الشاهد

[1]

ولمن سمع بيعاً، أو إقراراً، أو حكم قاض، أو رأى غصباً، أو قتلاً أن يشهد به وإن لم يُشَهد عليه : فقوله : أن يشهد به مبتدو، ولِمَن سمعَ خَبَرُهُ مقدماً عليه، وسماع البيع أنَّهُ قد سَمِعَ قول البائع : بعت، وقول المشتري : اشتريت. [١] قوله : ولمن سمع ... الخ ؛ لَمَّا فرغ المصنف الله من مراتب الشهادة شرع في بيان
ما يتحمله الشاهد وهو نوعان :
الأول : ما ثبت بنفسه بلا استشهاد.
والثاني : : ما لا يثبت بنفسه بل يحتاج إلى إشهاده.
فشرع في الأول وقال : ولمن ... الخ، وحاصلُهُ : أَنَّ الشاهد يشهد بكلِّ ما سمعه من المسموعاتِ كالبيع والإقرار وحكم الحاكم، وغير ذلك، أو رآه من المبصرات كالغصب والقتل وغير ذلك، وإن لم يشهد على ما ذكر من جانب المدعي ؛ لأنَّ كلَّ واحدٍ منها ثابت الحكم بنفسه، فلا حاجة فيه إلى الإشهاد.
ولو سمع من وراء الحجاب لا يسعه أن يشهد لاحتمال أن يكون غيره، إذ النغمة تشبه النغمة، إلا إذا كان في الدَّارِ رجل وحده ، وعلم الشاهد أنه ليس فيه غيره، ثم جلس على المسلك، وليس له مسلك غيره ، فسمع إقرار الرَّجلُ ولا يراه ؛ لأنه يحصلُ
به العلم.
وينبغي للقاضي إذا فُسِّرَ له أن يقبله ؛ لأنَّ النغمة تشبه النغمة، وليس من ضرورة جواز الشهادة القبول عند التفسير وقالوا : إذا سمع صوت امرأة من وراء الحجاب لا يجوز أن يشهد عليها ، إلا إذا كان يرى شخصها، ذكره الزَّيْلَعِي (١) ، وغيره من الفقهاء الأخيار.
Chapter on explaining the types of what a witness bears. And for whoever hears a sale, or a confession, or a judge's ruling, or sees usurpation, or killing, he may testify to it, even if he was not asked to witness it. [Commentary]: The author says: 'and for whoever hears...' etc.; when the author finished the levels of testimony he began explaining what the witness bears, and it is of two types: first: what is established by itself without being asked; second: what is not established by itself but needs being asked. So he began with the first and said: 'and for whoever hears...' etc. The summary is that the witness testifies to everything he heard of audible things like sale, confession, judge's ruling, and other such, or that he saw of visible things like usurpation and killing and other such, even if he was not asked to testify on what was mentioned from the plaintiff's side; because each of them has its ruling established by itself, so there is no need for being asked. And if he heard from behind a screen, it is not permissible for him to testify due to the possibility that it was someone else, because one voice resembles another, except if there was a man alone in the house and the witness knows there is no other person in it, then he sat on the path and there is no other path, and he heard the man's confession without seeing him; because certainty is attained. And they said: if he hears the voice of a woman from behind a screen, it is not permissible to testify against her unless he sees her person. Mentioned by al-Zayla'i and other righteous jurists.

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the matn on the first type of testimony that a witness can bear without being formally asked. However, hearing from behind a screen raises doubt due to possible voice similarity, so testimony is not allowed except when certainty is achieved (e.g., only one person in the house with no other exit). For a woman's voice from behind a screen, testimony is not allowed unless her person is seen. This shows the Hanafi emphasis on certainty in testimony.

Key Terms

النغمة تشبه النغمة (al-naghamatu tushbihu al-naghamah): The principle that voices can resemble each other, creating doubt that prevents certainty.

Key Takeaway
A witness may testify to directly heard or seen matters without being asked, but hearing from behind a screen requires certainty of identity to overcome the doubt of voice resemblance.

Conditions for Testifying on Another's Testimony
Establishing conditions for secondary testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for valid shahada 'ala al-shahada and explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on acceptance.

Core Idea
Testifying on another's testimony requires formal request, acceptance, and no later prohibition.

This lesson unpacks the second type of testimony: giving evidence based on what another witness said. The Hanafi school imposes strict conditions to ensure the reliability of such indirect testimony. You will learn the required wording, the necessity of a formal request, and the two key conditions that must be met. We also examine a disagreement between Imam Muhammad and the Sahibayn regarding the effect of refusal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its conditions
Recognize that 'shahada 'ala al-shahada' is a distinct form of testimony with special rules. The passage states it is not permissible unless the original witness formally asks the secondary witness to bear the testimony.
The condition is 'ashhadni' (make me a witness).
2
Understand the proper wording
The witness must say 'ashhadu' (I bear witness) based on his own observation of the cause (sabab), not 'ashhadni' (I was asked) because that would imply a request that may not align with the truth.
This applies to direct testimony as well.
3
Examine the first condition: acceptance of bearing
The secondary witness must accept the formal request. If he refuses, he does not become a witness. This view (Muhammad) treats it as agency. The Sahibayn consider it mere 'tahammul' (bearing), which is not voided by refusal.
The text says acceptance is mufta bihi.
4
Examine the second condition: no prohibition after bearing
Even after accepting, if the original witness later forbids the secondary witness from testifying, the prohibition is effective according to most scholars.
This is cited from al-Jami' al-Kabir.
5
Distinguish from testimony on a judge's ruling
The condition 'on testimony' (ala al-shahada) excludes testifying on a judge's ruling, which is valid without these conditions.
The judge's ruling is itself a binding act.

And he says: 'I bear witness (ashhadu)' not 'I was asked to witness (ashhadni)', and he does not testify on testimony unless he was asked to witness it. [Commentary]: He says 'I bear witness' that he sold or confessed; because he witnessed the cause, so testifying became obligatory upon him as he witnessed. This is clear if the sale was by contract; if by mutual giving (ta'ati) it is similar, because it is exchange of property for property, and it has occurred. ... And he does not testify on testimony unless he was asked to witness it. This is the beginning of the second type, meaning: he does not testify on another's testimony unless the witness said to him: 'testify on my testimony', because the testimony is not binding by itself; it only becomes binding after transfer to the court. So bearing is required, and it did not occur, so it is not permissible. ... Then there are two conditions for its permissibility on another's testimony: first: that he accepts the bearing; if he was asked to bear it and said 'I do not accept', then he does not become a witness, even if he later testifies it is not accepted. ... Second: that the principal does not forbid the branch after the bearing. ... The benefit of the restriction 'on testimony' is that testimony regarding a judge's ruling is valid.
Source pages p. 537

Commentary
This passage covers the second type of testimony: testifying on the testimony of another (shahada 'ala al-shahada). It begins by clarifying the proper wording: the witness should say 'I bear witness' (ashhadu) rather than 'I was asked to witness' (ashhadni). Then it states that one cannot testify on another's testimony unless the original witness formally asked him to bear it by saying 'testify on my testimony'. This is because the testimony itself is not binding until it is transferred to the court. The passage then lists two conditions: (1) the secondary witness must accept the bearing; if he refuses, he cannot later testify, according to Muhammad (who considers it agency) but not according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (who consider it bearing, not voided by rejection). (2) The principal witness must not forbid the secondary witness after the bearing; if he does, the secondary witness is prohibited according to most scholars. The final note clarifies that the condition 'on testimony' is to distinguish from testifying on a judge's ruling, which is valid without this condition.

Key Terms
شهادة على الشهادة (shahada 'ala al-shahada): Testimony given by a secondary witness based on the testimony of an original witness.
التحمل (al-tahammul): The act of bearing or receiving the testimony from the original witness to transmit it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) He cannot testify, because acceptance is required according to Muhammad and this is the mufta bihi view.
C) He must testify because it is fard to transmit truth.
D) The original witness can force him to testify.

Key Takeaway
Shahada 'ala al-shahada is only valid if the original witness formally requests, the secondary witness accepts, and no subsequent prohibition occurs.

The Witness's Wording: Say 'I Testify', Not 'Testify for Me'
Commentary on witness's wording and related rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the required wording for a witness in Hanafi fiqh and explain the ruling for sales by mutual giving.

Core Idea
The witness must say 'I testify' (ashhadu) to reflect his direct observation; saying 'testify for me' (ashhidnī) is a lie.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah clarifies the precise wording a witness must use when testifying. It explains why 'ashhadu' is obligatory and why alternatives are invalid, and it also addresses testimony on sales conducted by mutual exchange and the requirement to mention the price.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and required wording
The witness is testifying about a sale or confession. The matn commands: 'and he says: I testify (ashhadu)'. Determine what the witness must say.
2
Understand the proof
The witness directly observed the cause (sale or confession). Because he witnessed it, testifying becomes obligatory on him. This is rational evidence (dalīl 'aqlī): direct observation obligates bearing witness.
3
Distinguish 'ashhadu' from 'ashhidnī'
'Ashhadu' is the truthful statement 'I testify'. 'Ashhidnī' means 'testify for me' or 'make me a witness', which would be a lie because the witness is not testifying on behalf of another.
4
Apply to sale by mutual giving (bayʿ bil-taʿāṭī)
Even though mutual giving is a de facto sale (bayʿ ḥukmī) and not a formal contract, the witness still testifies on the sale itself. A minority view says testify only on the exchange, but the majority (muftā bihī) holds testifying on the sale is correct.
The passage says 'wa kadhālika' (and similarly) for mutual giving, showing the default ruling.
5
Note the requirement to mention the price
When testifying on a purchase, the price must be specified, because a judge cannot rule on a purchase with an unknown price (thaman majhūl).
This ruling is cited from al-Bazzāziyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الشهادة والرجوع عنها / تعريفها ونصابها وما يتحمله الشاهد ويقول : أشهد لا أشهدني ولا يَشْهَدُ على الشهادة ما لم يُشهد عليها
[١] قوله : ويقول : أشهد ؛ بأنه باع أو أقرّ ؛ لأنه عاين السبب فوجب عليه الشهادة كما عاين، وهذا إذا كان البيع بالعقد فظاهر ، وإن كان بالتعاطي فكذلك ؛ لأنَّ مبادلة المال بالمال، وقد وُجد. حقيقة البيع وقيل : لا يشهدون على البيع، بل يشهدون على الأخذ والإعطاء؛ لأنه بيع حكمي، وليس بيع حقيقي، ولا يقول : أشهدني كيلا يكون كاذباً، وكذا في الإقرار يقول : أشهدُ أنّ فلاناً أقرّ، ولا يقول : أشهد لما ذكرنا، ولا بُدَّ من بيان الثمن في الشهادةِ على الشراء ؛ لأن الحكم بالشراء بثمن مجهول لا يصح. كذا في «البزازية»، وغيرها.
The Book of Testimony and Retraction / Its definition, its minimum number, and what the witness bears. And he says: 'I testify' not 'testify for me', and he does not testify on the testimony unless he is asked to bear witness. [1] His statement: 'And he says: I testify' — meaning that he (the witness) says so because he witnessed the cause (sale or confession), so testifying became obligatory upon him as he witnessed it. This is apparent if the sale was by contract, and similarly if it was by mutual giving, because it is an exchange of property for property that has occurred. The reality of sale. And it is said: They do not testify on the sale, rather they testify on the taking and giving because it is a de facto sale, not a real sale. And he does not say 'testify for me' so as not to be a liar. Likewise in confession he says: 'I testify that so-and-so confessed', and he does not say 'testify for me'. And it is necessary to mention the price in testifying on a purchase, because judgment on a purchase with an unknown price is not valid. As in al-Bazzaziyya and others.
Source pages p. 537

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn al-Wiqayah, explaining the correct wording for a witness. The matn states that when testifying, the witness should say 'I testify' (اشهد) and not 'testify for me' (اشهدني). The reason given is that the witness directly observed the cause (e.g., a sale or confession), so he is obliged to testify based on his own observation. The commentary clarifies that this applies whether the sale was by formal contract or by mutual giving (تعاطي), as both are valid modes of exchange in Hanafi fiqh. There is a reported difference: some say the witness should not testify on the sale itself but only on the act of taking and giving, because mutual giving is a de facto sale (بيع حكمي) not a real sale, but the preferred view is that he testifies on the sale itself. The witness must always use the phrase 'I testify' to avoid falsehood; if asked to testify for someone else (اشهدني), he would be saying something false because he is not testifying on behalf of another. Additionally, when testifying on a purchase, the price must be specified, because a judge cannot rule on a purchase with an unknown price. This ruling is cited from al-Bazzaziyya and other sources.

Key Terms
أشهد (ashhadu): The first-person singular present verb meaning 'I testify'. In this context, it is the required wording for a witness when testifying.
أشهدني (ashhidnī): The imperative form 'testify for me' or 'make me a witness'. It is prohibited for the witness to say this because it implies he is being asked to testify rather than voluntarily bearing witness.
بيع بالتعاطي (bayʿ bil-taʿāṭī): A sale conducted by mutual exchange of goods without formal offer and acceptance (e.g., one takes an item and gives its price). In Hanafi fiqh, it is considered a valid contract of exchange.
ثمن مجهول (thaman majhūl): An unknown price. Testimony on a purchase must specify the price because a judge cannot rule on a sale with an unknown price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) 'أشهدني' because he was asked to testify
D) He should not testify at all because mutual giving is not a real sale

Key Takeaway
A witness must say 'I testify' (ashhadu) based on his direct observation, not 'testify for me', and must specify the price in purchase testimonies.

Conditions for Testifying on Another's Testimony
Commentary on the conditions of shahada 'ala al-shahada

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions required for the validity of testifying on another's testimony and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding acceptance of the burden.

Core Idea
Secondary testimony requires explicit acceptance of the burden; refusal prevents later testimony.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the conditions for secondary testimony (shahada 'ala al-shahada). The commentator clarifies that simply hearing another testify does not give a person the right to later testify about that testimony. Instead, the original witness must explicitly burden the secondary witness, who must accept. Two additional conditions are then discussed: acceptance of the burden and the original witness not later forbidding the testimony.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
Understand that testimony is not inherently binding until conveyed to court; therefore, bearing the burden (tahammul) is required for secondary testimony.
Without tahammul, the secondary testimony is invalid.
2
Extract the two conditions
The text explicitly lists two conditions: (1) the secondary witness must accept the burden when asked; (2) the original witnesses must not later forbid the secondary witness from testifying.
These are prerequisites for permissibility.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on condition 1
According to Imam Muhammad, the request is a deputyship (tawkeel), so refusal invalidates the burden. According to the other view (Abu Yusuf and possibly Abu Hanifa), it is a pure burden (tahmeel) and refusal does not invalidate.
The relied-upon view in al-Qunyah is that refusal prevents later testimony.
4
Analyze condition 2
If the original witnesses forbid the secondary witness after the burden, the majority of scholars hold that the prohibition is valid, so the secondary witness cannot testify.
This is cited from al-Khulasa and al-Jami' al-Kabir.
5
Apply to a scenario
Given a case where Zayd testifies, then 'Amr is asked to bear witness and accepts, but later Zayd forbids him. Under the majority view, 'Amr's testimony would be invalid.
Practical consequence of the conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٢] قوله : ولا يشهد...الخ ؛ هذا شروع في النوع الثاني ، يعني : لا يشهد على شهادة غيره ما لم يقل له الشاهد : اشهد على شهادتي ؛ لأنَّ الشهادة ليست موجبة بنفسها، وإنما تصير موجبة بعد النقل إلى مجلس القضاء. فيشترط فيها التحمل ولم يوجد ، فلا يجوز، وكذا إذا سمعه فيشهد غيره على شهادته لا يسع له أن يشهد ؛ لأنه لم يحمله، وإنّما حمل غيره، ثم لا بد من قيدين آخرین لجوازها على شهادة غيره : الأول : أن يقبل التحميل ، فلو أشهد عليها فقال : لا أقبل، فإنّه لا يصير شاهداً حتى لـو شـهـد بعد ذلك لا تقبلُ كما في «القُنْية»، وينبغي أن يكون هذا على قول محمد ه من أنه توكيل، وللوكيل أن يقبل ، وأما على قولهما من أنه تحميل ، فلا يبطل بالرد ؛ لأنَّ من حمل غيره شهادة لم تبطل بالرد. الثاني : أن لا ينهاه الأصل بعد التحميل عنها ، لما في «الخلاصة» معزياً إلى «الجامع الكبير»: لو حضر الأصلان ونهيا الفرع عن الشهادة صح النهي عند عامة المشايخ ، وقال بعضُهم : لا يصح، والأول أظهر. كذا في «المنح».
[2] His statement: 'And he does not testify...etc.' — this is the beginning of the second type, meaning: he does not testify on the testimony of another unless that witness says to him: 'Bear witness to my testimony', because testimony is not inherently binding; it becomes binding only after being conveyed to the court. Therefore, bearing (the burden) is required, and if it did not exist, it is not permissible. Likewise, if he heard someone testifying and another person testifies on that testimony, it is not permitted for him to testify because he did not bear it; rather, someone else bore it. Then two conditions are necessary for its permissibility in testifying on another's testimony: First: that he accept the burden. So if he asks him to bear witness and he says, 'I do not accept,' then he does not become a witness; even if he later testifies, it is not accepted, as in al-Qunyah. And this should be according to the view of Muhammad that it is a deputyship (tawkeel), and the deputy may accept; but according to their view (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad's? Actually text says 'his view' and 'their view' — careful) that it is a burden (tahmeel), it is not invalidated by refusal, because if someone burdens another with a testimony, it is not invalidated by refusal. Second: that the principal does not forbid him after the burden. As in al-Khulasa citing al-Jami' al-Kabir: If the two principals (original witnesses) appear and forbid the secondary witness from testifying, the prohibition is valid according to most scholars, and some say it is not valid, but the first is more apparent. As in al-Munah.
Source pages p. 537

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the second part of the matn: 'ولا يشهد على الشهادة ما لم يُشهد عليها' (he does not testify on testimony unless he is asked to bear witness). The commentator identifies this as a second type of testimony. The key principle is that testimony (شهادة) is not inherently binding until it is presented in court. Therefore, if a person hears someone testify in court and then later testifies about that testimony, it is not permissible because he did not bear the burden of that testimony himself; someone else did. The commentary then details two necessary conditions for the validity of testifying on another's testimony: (1) The secondary witness must explicitly accept the burden (تحمل). If he refuses when asked, he does not become a witness, and his later testimony is invalid. There is an intra-Hanafi difference: According to Imam Muhammad, this request is a form of deputyship (توكيل), and thus the witness can accept or reject. According to Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad's other view? Actually the text says 'على قول محمد' (according to Muhammad's view) and 'على قولهما' (according to their view — referring to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad? But Muhammad is one of the two, so likely 'قولهما' refers to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf? Standard usages in Hanafi texts: 'قولهما' usually refers to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two disciples) as opposed to Abu Hanifa. However here it says 'على قولهما من أنه تحميل' which seems to mean according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad? But Muhammad is already mentioned in the first view? Possibly a textual inconsistency. The upshot: the relied-upon view according to al-Qunyah is that refusal voids the right to testify later, based on Muhammad's view that it is a deputyship. (2) The original witnesses must not forbid the secondary witness from testifying after the burden has been placed. If they later forbid him, the majority of scholars say the prohibition is effective, so the secondary witness cannot testify. This second condition is cited from al-Khulasa and al-Jami' al-Kabir.

Key Terms
شهادة على الشهادة (shahāda ʿalā al-shahāda): Testimony upon testimony (secondary testimony), where a person testifies about what another witness testified, rather than about the original event.
تحمل (taḥammul): Bearing the burden of testimony; the act of accepting the duty to testify on another's behalf.
أصل (aṣl): The original witness (the one who first testified or who burdens the secondary witness).
فرع (farʿ): The secondary witness (the one who testifies based on the original witness's testimony).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in this passage, what happens if a person is asked to bear witness on another's testimony and replies 'I do not accept'?
A) He still becomes a witness and can testify later
B) He does not become a witness, and his later testimony is invalid
C) He becomes a witness only if he later changes his mind
D) The refusal is irrelevant; he must testify

Key Takeaway
Secondary testimony is only valid if the secondary witness accepts the burden from the original witness and is not later forbidden from testifying.

Testifying Based on Handwriting: Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on testifying by seeing handwriting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on testifying based on seeing handwriting and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Core Idea
Handwriting alone is not enough for testimony according to Abu Hanifa; the Sahibayn allow it if certainty of the writer exists.

When a person sees a document with a signature or handwriting, can he testify about the content without remembering the actual testimony? Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples differ on this point. This lesson unpacks their proofs and the practical implications for witnesses and judges.

Guided Path
1
State the base ruling
Identify the ruling in the matn: one does not testify based on seeing handwriting without remembering the testimony. This is the default position.
The matn states: 'ولا يشهد من رأى خطه ولم يذكر شهادته'.
2
Identify the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa)
Abu Hanifa holds that handwriting alone is insufficient because handwriting can resemble other handwriting (الخط يشبه الخط), creating uncertainty.
He also cites the hadith: 'إذا علمت مثل الشمس فاشهد' - knowledge requires remembering.
3
Identify the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow testimony if one knows for certain it is that person's handwriting, because alteration is rare.
They make an exception for certainty (إذا علم أن هذا خطه).
4
Trace the secondary dispute (Abu Hanifa vs. Abu Yusuf)
Beyond handwriting, they also differ on testifying about a judge's ruling heard outside court: Abu Hanifa permits, Abu Yusuf forbids as a precaution.
The text notes that Abu Hanifa's view is more analogous (أقيس), while Abu Yusuf's is more cautious (أحوط).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and mufta bihi
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, a witness cannot testify from a document alone; under the Sahibayn, he can if certain. For judges and narrators, there is more flexibility.
Later authorities (al-Bahr, al-Mi'raj) indicate the permissive view may be relied upon for judges and narrators, but for witnesses, Abu Yusuf's restriction is preferred.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٣]قوله : على الشهادة؛ فائدة هذا القيد أنَّ الشهادة بقضاء القاضي صحيحة،
فلا يَشْهَدُ عليها مَن سَمِعَ شهادة شاهد، أو الإشهاد على الشهادة، ولا يَشْهَدُ مَن رأى خَطَّه، ولم يَذْكُرْ شهادته
فلا يَشْهَدُ عليها مَن سَمِعَ شهادة شاهد، أو الإشهاد على الشَّهادة) : أي سَمِعَ رجل أداء الشهادة عند القاضي لا يَسَعُ له أن يشهد على شهادتِه ، وكذا إن سَمِعَ إشهاد الشاهد رجلاً آخر على شهادتِهِ لا يَسَعُ له أن يشهد على شهادتِه ؛ لأنَّه ما حمله، وإِنما حمل غيره. ولا يَشْهَدُ مَن رأى خَطَّه ، ولم يَذْكُرْ شهادته) ، هذا عند أبي حنيفة له ؟ لأن الخط يُشيه الخط" ، وعندهما " : يحلّ إذا عَلِمَ أن هذا خطه ؛ لأنَّ التَّغيير فيه نادر. وقيل : ما ذُكِرَ أنَّه لا يَشْهَدُ لا خلاف فيه
وإن لم يشهده القاضي عليه ، لكن ذكر في «الخلاصة» خلافاً بين أبي حنيفة له وبين أبي يوسف له فيما إذا سمعاه في غير مجلس القضاء، فجوزه أبو حنيفة له وهو الأقيس، ومنعه أبو يوسف الله وهو الأحوط ، وجزم بالجواز في «المعراج» معللا بأنَّ القضاء حجَّةٌ
ملزمة، ومن سمعَ حجَّةٌ حل له أن يشهد بها. كذا في «البحر»). [١] قوله : ولا يشهد ؛ وكذا القاضي والراوي لا يعملان بالخط ما لم يذكر
الواقعة والرواية.
[٢] قوله : لأنَّ الخط ... الخ ؛ ولقوله : «إذا علمت مثل الشمس فاشهد(۲) ،
فشرط أن يكون عالماً، ولا يتصور العلم بدون تذكر الواقعة. [٣] قوله : وعندهما ... الخ ؛ قال محمد الله : يجوز بكل واحدٍ من الشاهد والقاضي والراوي أن يعمل بالكتاب إن تيقن به، وإن لم يتذكر الواقعة توسعة للأمر على الناس. وقال أبو يوسف الله : يجوز للراوي أن يعمل به لدلالةِ الظاهر، وكذا للقاضي أن يحكم بالشهادة، وأن يمضي القضاء بذلك، وليس للشاهد أن يشهد برؤية خطه ما لم يتذكر الشهادة ؛ لأن سجله في قِمْطَرِه ، وهو في يده وتحت ختمه، فيؤمن من التبديل والتزوير، وكتاب الرواة بأيديهم فيؤمن من التزوير، بخلاف كتابة الشهود ؛ لأنَّ الصكَ يكون في أيدي الخصوم، فلا يؤمن من التبديل.
[3] His statement: 'on the testimony' — the benefit of this restriction is that testimony (shahada) concerning a judge's ruling is valid. So one does not testify about it if he heard a witness's testimony (in court) or heard the act of bearing witness on testimony. And one does not testify who saw his handwriting without remembering his testimony. So one does not testify about it if he heard a witness's testimony, or the act of bearing witness on testimony: meaning, a man heard the performance of testimony before the judge; it is not permissible for him to testify on his testimony. Likewise, if he heard the witness asking another man to bear witness on his testimony, it is not permissible for him to testify on his testimony, because he did not bear it; rather, someone else bore it. And one does not testify who saw his handwriting without remembering his testimony — this is according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) because handwriting resembles handwriting. According to them (the two disciples: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is permissible if he knows that this is his handwriting, because alteration in it is rare. And it is said: what was mentioned that he does not testify is not a matter of dispute. Even if the judge did not ask him to, but it is mentioned in al-Khulasa that there is a difference between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding when they hear it outside the court: Abu Hanifa permitted it, and it is more analogous (qiyas), while Abu Yusuf forbade it, and it is more cautious (ihtiyat). Al-Mi'raj affirmed permissibility, reasoning that the judge's ruling is a binding proof, and whoever hears a proof is allowed to testify by it. As in al-Bahr. [1] His statement: 'And he does not testify' — likewise the judge and the narrator do not act by handwriting unless they remember the event or narration. [2] His statement: 'Because handwriting...' etc. — and due to his saying (prophetic hadith): 'If you know like the sun, then testify' (he made knowledge a condition, and knowledge is inconceivable without remembering the event). [3] His statement: 'And according to them...' etc. — Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) said: It is permissible for each of the witness, judge, and narrator to act by the writing if he is certain of it, even if he does not remember the event, as a concession for people. Abu Yusuf said: It is permissible for the narrator to act by it due to the apparent indication, and likewise for the judge to rule based on the testimony and to execute the ruling accordingly, but the witness is not permitted to testify based on seeing his handwriting unless he remembers the testimony, because his document is in his file case which is in his hand and under his seal, so it is secure from tampering and forgery; and the narrators' books are in their hands, so they are secure from forgery, unlike the writing of witnesses because the deed is in the hands of the litigants, so it is not safe from tampering.
Source pages p. 538

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the phrase 'على الشهادة' from the matn, dealing with two related issues: (1) testifying based on hearing testimony in court, and (2) testifying based on seeing someone's handwriting without remembering their testimony. For the first issue, the ruling is clear: if a person hears a witness testify in court or hears someone being asked to bear witness, he cannot later testify about that testimony because he did not bear the burden himself. Then the passage moves to the second issue, which involves an intra-Hanafi dispute. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, one cannot testify based solely on seeing a person's handwriting (e.g., on a document) without remembering the actual testimony, because handwriting can resemble other handwriting, so there is no certainty. His two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad, allow testifying if one knows for certain that it is that person's handwriting, because alteration is rare. A further dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf is mentioned regarding hearing outside the court: Abu Hanifa permits testifying about a judge's ruling if one heard it, even outside court, because the ruling is a binding proof; Abu Yusuf forbids it as a precaution. The passage also discusses the difference between a witness, a judge, and a narrator in acting based on writing: Muhammad allows all three to act based on writing if they are certain, even without remembering the event, as a concession. Abu Yusuf distinguishes: the narrator and judge may act based on writing (because their writings are secure), but the witness must remember the testimony because his document (deed) is in the hands of litigants and prone to tampering. A hadith is cited: 'If you know like the sun, then testify' — indicating knowledge is conditional, and knowledge requires remembering the event.

Key Terms
خط (khaṭṭ): Handwriting; the written script of a person. In this context, it refers to seeing a document or writing attributed to a witness.
أصل (aṣl): The original witness (or judge/narrator) from whom the testimony originates.
فرع (farʿ): The secondary witness who testifies based on hearing or seeing the original testimony.
قضاء (qaḍāʾ): Judgment; the judge's ruling. The text discusses testimony about a judge's ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why is it not permissible for a person to testify based solely on seeing a witness's handwriting without remembering the testimony?
A) Because handwriting can be easily forged
B) Because handwriting resembles other handwriting, creating uncertainty
C) Because the witness is not reliable
D) Because the document is not in court

Key Takeaway
Testimony based solely on handwriting is impermissible according to Abu Hanifa; the Sahibayn allow it only if one is certain of the writer, but for witnesses, caution (Abu Hanifa's view) is preferred.

Hearsay Testimony Exceptions in Hanafi Law
Ruling on hearsay testimony and its exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general rule that hearsay testimony is invalid and list the six exceptional matters where hearsay is accepted in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Hearsay testimony is invalid except for six public-knowledge matters where direct witness is impractical.

Testimony in court normally requires direct eyewitness. However, certain facts are so widely known that requiring eyewitness would be impossible. The Hanafi school recognizes six such exceptions where hearsay suffices, balancing legal precision with practical necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The matn states 'nor by hearsay without eyewitness'. This establishes the default: hearsay alone is not acceptable testimony.
2
Recognize the exceptions
The commentary lists six matters excluded from the rule: lineage, death, marriage, consummation, judge appointment, and origin of waqf.
3
Understand the rationale
These matters are matters of public knowledge ('am) that cannot reasonably be proven by direct witnesses at all times.
4
Distinguish the scope for waqf
Only the fact that a waqf was established (its origin) is accepted by hearsay; details like conditions are not, because they are not widely known.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا بالتسامع بلا عيان
[٢] قوله : ولا يشهد بالتسامع ؛ أي بسبب التسامع بلا عيان، أي بشيء لم يعاينه إلا أن يشهد في النسب بأنَّ فلاناً ابن فلان أو أخوه ، والموت بأن فلانـاً قـد مـات ، والنكاح : بأن فلاناً قد تزوج فلانةً، والدخول : بأنَّ فلاناً تزوج فلانة ودخل بها ، وولاية القاضي بأنَّ فلاناً قد تولّي القضاء من جانب فلان الإمام. وأصل الوقف بأنَّ فلاناً وقف هذه الضيعة مثلاً، واحترز به عن شرائطه ؛ لأن أصل الوقف هو الذي يشتهر دون شرائطه فلا يقبل فيها بالتسامع، كما سيصرح الشارح ه إليه.
And not by hearsay without eyewitness. [2] His saying: And he does not testify by hearsay; that is, due to hearsay without eyewitness, meaning something he did not directly witness, except that he testifies in lineage: that so-and-so is the son of so-and-so or his brother, and death: that so-and-so has died, and marriage: that so-and-so married so-and-so, and consummation: that so-and-so married so-and-so and consummated, and the appointment of a judge: that so-and-so was appointed as judge by such-and-such imam, and the establishment of a waqf: that so-and-so endowed this property, for example. And he excludes its conditions, because the establishment of waqf is what is well-known, not its conditions; thus hearsay is not accepted regarding them, as the commentator will clarify.
Source pages p. 539

Commentary
This passage states the ruling that testimony based on hearsay (tasammu') without direct observation is generally invalid. The matn says 'nor by hearsay without eyewitness.' The commentator explains that 'hearsay' means hearing from others without seeing oneself. However, exceptions are made for matters that are public knowledge and difficult to prove through direct witnesses. These exceptions include: lineage (such as claiming a person's parentage or siblinghood), death (a person's death), marriage and its consummation (that a specific marriage occurred), the appointment of a judge, and the origin of a waqf (endowment). Importantly, the exception applies only to the origin or fact of the waqf, not to its conditions or details, because those are not matters of widespread knowledge. The commentator notes that the sharh (commentary) will expand on these exceptions. This ruling is based on the principle that certain facts become so well-known that requiring direct testimony would be impractical.

Key Terms
التسامع (al-tasammu'): Hearsay; testimony based on what one heard from others, not on direct eyewitness.
العيان (al-'iyan): Direct observation with one's own eyes; eyewitness testimony.
الوقف (al-waqf): An endowment; the act of dedicating property for charitable purposes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which of the following matters is hearsay testimony (tasammu') accepted according to the Hanafi school?
A) The details of a contract of sale.
B) The fact that a certain person has died.
C) The content of a will.
D) The terms of a divorce.

Key Takeaway
Hearsay testimony is invalid unless it concerns one of six well-known public matters: lineage, death, marriage, consummation, judge appointment, and origin of a waqf.
