Shahadat — Testimony
intermediate
10 min
Conditions for Testifying Ownership Based on Possession
Explains when yad (possession) is evidence of ownership and exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a witness may testify to ownership based on possession and identify the exception for slaves.

Core Idea
Possession is evidence of ownership only if it generates inner conviction, and never for a speaking human.

Testimony is a serious matter in Islamic law. When a witness observes someone in possession of an object, can they testify that the object belongs to that person? The Hanafi school sets clear conditions: possession must create a genuine presumption of ownership in the witness's heart, and some objects – namely, human beings – are entirely exempt. This lesson unpacks these rules, the intra-Hanafi dispute, and the rebuttal of opposing views.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule and its condition
From the text: one may testify that something in the hand of a disposer (mutasarrif) belongs to him. But note: the witness must have an inner presumption (zann) of ownership; mere sight of the hand is insufficient.
2
Recognize the exception for slaves
A slave is excluded because a human has a hand over himself – his own testimony about his freedom is accepted, so another's possession does not prove ownership.
This applies to any free person who can express themselves; minors are treated as property.
3
Understand the effect of hearsay or contrary information
If the witness qualifies his testimony as based on hearsay (tasamu') or mere hand, it is invalid. Also, if two just witnesses inform him the item belongs to another, he cannot testify based on possession.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa reportedly allowed testimony even for slaves; the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) required inner presumption. The mufta bihi is the view of the two companions as per al-Sadr al-Shahid and al-Razi.
5
Evaluate the cross-madhhab disagreement
Al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ibn Hamid require both hand and actual disposal (tasarruf). The Hanafi response: disposal includes original and proxy forms, so the original hand suffices.
The author's restriction 'mutasarrif' is for agreement, but the Hanafi position does not require explicit disposal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشيء سوى الرقيق في يد متصرف كالملاك أنه له
وأسند هذا القيد في «الظهيرية» إلى الصاحبين ، قالوا : يحتمل أن يكون تفسيراً لإطلاق محمد الله في الرواية، قال الصدر الشهيد : يحتمل أن يكون قوله قول الكل، وبه يأخذ. وقال الرازي : هذا قولهم جميعاً ، إذ الأصلُ في حل الشهادة اليقين، فعند تعدرِهِ يصار إلى ما يشهد له القلب ؛ لأنَّ كونَ اليدِ مسوغاً بسبب إفادتها ظنّ الملك ، فإذا لم يقع في القلب ذلك الظنّ لم يفد مجرَّد اليد، ولهذا قالوا : إذا رأى إنسان درة ثمينة في يد كناس، أو كتاباً في يد جاهل ليس في آبائه من هو أهل له لا يسعه أن يشهد بالملك له ، فعُرِفَ أن مجرَّدَ اليد لا يكفي. ويشترط أن لا يخبره عدلان بأنها لغيره، فلو أخبراه لم يجز له الشهادة بالملك ، بخلاف ما إذا شهد به عدل واحد ؛ لأنَّ شهادة الواحد لا تزيلُ ما كان في قلبك أنه للأول، فلا يحل لك أن تمتنع عن الشهادة إلا أن يقع في قلبك أن هذا الواحد صادق، فحينئذ لا يحل لك أن تشهد أنه للأوّل، وزيادة التفصيل. كذا في «حاشية الطحطاوي على الدر المختار» إن شئت فارجع إليها.
قوله : سوى الرقيق ؛ وعن أبي حنيفة له : إنه يحل له أن يشهد فيه أيضا، وهو رواية عن أبي يوسف ومحمد. كذا في «شرح البرجندي».
قوله : متصرف ؛ لم يقيد بهذا القيد صاحب «الهداية»، و«الكنز»، وقال الخصافُ : إن دليل الملك اليد مع التصرف، وهو قول الشافعي ومالك وابن حامد الحنبلي .
فإن فسَّرَ للقاضي شهادتَهُ بالتّسامع ، أو بحكم اليد بطلت
قلنا : التصرُّف يتنوع أيضا إلى أصالة وهي ظاهرة وسبابة، كالوكيل والمضارب وغيرهما، وانضمام محتمل إلى محتمل يزيد الاحتمال، فاعتبرنا أصـل اليد، ولعـل المصنف إنّما قيد بهذا القيد ؛ ليتحقق دليل الملكِ بالاتفاق.
وإنما قال سوى الرقيق ؛ لأنَّ الآدمي له يدٌ على نفسه فيدفعُ يد الغيرِ عن نفسه، والمراد إنسانٌ يُعبر عن نفسه حتَّى لو لم يُعبر عن نفسه كالصغير والصغيرة، فإنَّهما لا يد لهما فيعتبر يد الغير.
فإن فسر للقاضي شهادته بالتّسامع ، أو بحكم اليد بطلت
قوله : لأنَّ الآدمي له يد على نفسه ؛ حتى إذا ادّعى أنه حرُّ الأصل كان القولُ قوله، فلا يثبت لغيره عليه يد على الحقيقة، حتى يعتبر لإطلاق الشهادة بالملك ، ولا يمكن أن يعتبر فيه التصرف، وهو الاستخدام ؛ لأنَّ الحرّ أيضاً يستخدم طائعاً كالعبدِ فلا يصلح دليلاً على الملك.
قوله : إنسان يعبر عن نفسه ؛ هذا تفسير للكبير الواقع في عبارتهم، سواء كان ذكراً أو أنثى كما في «النهاية»، والوجه فيه : أنَّ لهما يداً على أنفسها تدفع يد الغير عنهما ، فانعدم دليل الملك، حتى لو ادَّعيا الحريَّة الأصلية يكون القول قولهما، وأمَّا الصغير الذي لا يعبر فهو كالمتاع لا يد له ، فله أن يشهد بالملك فيه لذي اليد.
قوله : بطلت ؛ ولا تقبل ؛ لأنَّ التسامع أو الرؤية في اليد يجوز للشهادة بالملك ، والقاضي يلزمه القضاء بالملك بالشهادة إذا كانت عن عيان ومشاهدة، أو إطلاق ؛ لاحتمال المشاهدة، فيحمل عليه.
And something other than a slave in the hand of a disposer, like ownership, that it is his. And this restriction is attributed in al-Zahiriyya to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). They said: It is possible that it is an interpretation of the absolute statement of Muhammad in the narration. Al-Sadr al-Shahid said: It is possible that his statement is the statement of all, and he takes it. And al-Razi said: This is their collective statement, because the basis for permissible testimony is certainty, and when it is impossible, recourse is made to what the heart testifies to; because the hand is a justification due to it providing a presumption of ownership, so if that presumption does not occur in the heart, mere hand is of no use. For this reason they said: If a person sees a precious pearl in the hand of a sweeper, or a book in the hand of an ignorant person whose ancestors were not worthy of it, it is not permissible for him to testify to ownership for him. So it is known that mere hand is not sufficient. And it is a condition that two just persons do not inform him that it belongs to another, for if they inform him, it is not permissible for him to testify to ownership, unlike if one just person testifies to that; because the testimony of one does not remove what is in your heart that it belongs to the first, so it is not permissible for you to refrain from testifying unless it occurs in your heart that this one is truthful, then it is not permissible for you to testify that it belongs to the first. And further detail is in Hashiyat al-Tahtawi 'ala al-Durr al-Mukhtar, if you wish, refer to it. His statement: 'other than a slave'; and from Abu Hanifa it is related that it is permissible for him to testify also regarding it, and it is a narration from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. As in Sharh al-Barjandi. His statement: 'disposer'; the author of al-Hidayah and al-Kanz did not restrict with this restriction, and al-Khassaf said: The evidence of ownership is the hand along with disposal, and it is the statement of al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ibn Hamid al-Hanbali. For if he explains to the judge his testimony as based on hearsay or by the ruling of the hand, it becomes invalid. We say: Disposal also varies into original and apparent and causatory, like the agent, the mudarib, and others, and the joining of a probable to a probable increases probability, so we considered the original hand, and perhaps the author only restricted with this restriction to verify the evidence of ownership by agreement. And he only said 'other than a slave' because a human has a hand over himself, so it repels the hand of another from himself. And what is meant is a person who can express himself, so if he does not express himself like a minor boy and girl, they have no hand, so the hand of another is considered. For if he explains to the judge his testimony as based on hearsay or by the ruling of the hand, it becomes invalid. His statement: 'because a human has a hand over himself'; so if he claims that he is originally free, his statement is accepted, and no hand of another over him is established in reality, so that testimony of ownership based on that is not considered valid, and disposal (i.e., use) cannot be considered in him because a free person also uses voluntarily like a slave, so it is not suitable as evidence of ownership. His statement: 'a person who can express himself'; this is an interpretation of 'adult' occurring in their expression, whether male or female as in al-Nihayah, and the rationale is that they have a hand over themselves that repels the hand of another from them, so the evidence of ownership is absent, so if they claim original freedom, their statement is accepted. As for a minor who cannot express himself, he is like a commodity, he has no hand, so it is permissible for him to testify to ownership for the possessor of the hand. His statement: 'it becomes invalid'; and it is not accepted, because hearsay or seeing in the hand is permissible for testimony of ownership, and the judge is obligated to judge ownership based on testimony if it is from direct observation and seeing, or from absolute expression because of the possibility of observation, so it is interpreted accordingly.
Source pages p. 543

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on the conditions under which a witness may testify to another's ownership of an object based on possession (yad). The matn states that one may testify that something in the hand of a person who has the ability to dispose of it (mutasarrif) belongs to that person, except if the object is a slave. The commentary expands on this: possession is only evidence if it generates a presumption of ownership in the witness's heart; mere hand without inner conviction is insufficient. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) are reported to have required this inner presumption, while there is a narration from Abu Hanifa allowing testimony even for slaves. The commentator explains that the exception for slaves is because a free person has control over himself and his testimony regarding his own freedom is accepted, so another's claim of ownership over him is not supported by mere possession. The passage also addresses the effect of two just witnesses informing the witness that the property belongs to someone else – in that case, the witness cannot testify based on possession. Lastly, if the witness explicitly states that his testimony is based on hearsay or mere possession, the testimony is invalid. The Hanafi position is that testimony should be absolute (i.e., not qualified) to carry weight.

Key Terms
يد (yad): Possession or control over an object, used as evidence of ownership in testimony.
متصرف (mutasarrif): One who has the ability to dispose of the property, either as owner or as authorized agent.
رقيق (raqiq): Slave, specifically a human being who is owned; exempted from the general rule because a person has control over himself.
تسامع (tasamu'): Hearsay; testifying based on what one has heard from others, not direct knowledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, can a witness testify that a slave belongs to the person holding him based solely on possession?
A) Yes, always.
B) No, because a slave has no hand over himself, and possession alone is not evidence.
C) Yes, but only if the witness has inner conviction.
D) No, because the slave himself can testify.

Key Takeaway
Testimony based on possession requires inner presumption of ownership; slaves are exempt, and hearsay invalidates the testimony.

Invalidity of Testimony Based on Hearsay or Possession
Ruling on invalidity when witness qualifies testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that testimony based on hearsay or possession is invalid and distinguish it from valid testimony based on direct observation.

Core Idea
Testimony must be based on certain knowledge; hearsay or mere possession does not suffice, making such testimony invalid.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah establishes a foundational rule in Hanafi testimony law: a witness who qualifies his testimony as based on hearsay or on mere possession (yad) invalidates it. The reason is that valid shahadah requires 'ilm al-yaqin (certain knowledge), which only direct observation provides. The commentary expands on why possession alone is insufficient, especially in cases of free persons and minors. Understanding this ruling clarifies the high evidentiary standard required in Hanafi fiqh for testimony concerning rights of people.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Recognize that the ruling applies when a witness explicitly qualifies his testimony as based on hearsay (tasamu') or possession (hukm al-yad). The ruling is that the testimony is invalid (batil).
2
Understand the basis
Testimony requires certain knowledge ('ilm al-yaqin). Hearsay and possession only provide probability (zann), which is insufficient. The witness' qualification admits lack of certainty.
3
Analyze the 'talil' reasoning on yad
The commentary explains why mere possession is not proof of ownership: a free person has possession over himself, so yad does not establish ownership. Disposition (usage) also does not prove ownership because a free person can be used willingly.
4
Distinguish absolute vs qualified testimony
Absolute (unqualified) testimony is accepted because it may be based on observation. Qualification reveals the witness lacks certainty, thus invalid.
5
Apply to scenarios
Consider the capacity of the subject: adults who can express themselves have possession over themselves, so yad is not proof; minors without expression are like goods, so yad may be evidence for the possessor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن فسَّرَ للقاضي شهادتَهُ بالتّسامع ، أو بحكم اليد بطلت (۳)(۱)
قلنا : التصرُّف يتنوع أيضا إلى أصالة وهي ظاهرة وسبابة، كالوكيل والمضارب وغيرهما، وانضمام محتمل إلى محتمل يزيد الاحتمال، فاعتبرنا أصـل اليد، ولعــل المصنف إنّما قيد بهذا القيد ؛ ليتحقق دليل الملكِ بالاتفاق.
[١) قوله : لأنَّ الآدمي له يد على نفسه ؛ حتى إذا ادَّعى أنه حرُّ الأصل كان القولُ قوله، فلا يثبت لغيره عليه يد على الحقيقة، حتى يعتبر لإطلاق الشهادة بالملك ، ولا يمكن أن يعتبر فيه التصرف، وهو الاستخدام ؛ لأنَّ الحرّ أيضاً يستخدم طائعاً كالعبدِ فلا يصلح دليلاً على الملك. [٢]قوله : إنسان يعبر عن نفسه ؛ هذا تفسير للكبير الواقع في عبارتهم، سواء كان ذكراً أو أنثى كما في «النهاية»، والوجه فيه : أنَّ لهما يداً على أنفسها تدفع يد الغير عنهما ، فانعدم دليل الملك، حتى لو ادَّعيا الحريَّة الأصلية يكون القول قولهما، وأمَّا الصغير الذي لا يعبر فهو كالمتاع لا يد له ، فله أن يشهد بالملك فيه لذي اليد.
[٣] قوله : بطلت ؛ ولا تقبل ؛ لأنَّ التسامع أو الرؤية في اليد يجوز للشهادة بالملك ، والقاضي يلزمه القضاء بالملك بالشهادة إذا كانت عن عيان ومشاهدة، أو إطلاق ؛ لاحتمال المشاهدة، فيحمل عليه.
(1) أي ينبغي للشاهد أن يطلق في أداء الشهادة لا يقول : إنها بالتسامع أو بحكم اليد ؛ لأنه يكون قد أقر بأنه شهد بغير علم ؛ ولأن القاضي إنما يلزم بالشهادة إذا كانت عن عيان أو عن إطلاق لاحتمالها المشاهدة فيحمل عليها. ينظر : فتح باب العناية (٣: ١٣٥).
If he interprets to the judge his testimony as based on hearsay or by the rule of possession, it is invalid. We say: Disposition also varies into principal, which is apparent and figurative, like the agent, the mudarib, and others. Combining a probable with a probable increases probability, so we considered the origin of possession. Perhaps the author only qualified with this qualifier to establish the proof of ownership by consensus. [1] His statement: 'Because the human being has possession over himself' – such that if he claims that he is originally free, his statement is accepted, so no one else's possession over him is established in reality, so that it would be considered to allow absolute testimony of ownership. And it is not possible to consider disposition (using him) as evidence because a free person also uses willingly like a slave, so it is not suitable as proof of ownership. [2] His statement: 'A person who expresses himself' – this is an interpretation of the adult in their expression, whether male or female as in al-Nihayah. The reason is that they have possession over themselves that repels the possession of others over them, so the proof of ownership is absent. Even if they claim original freedom, their statement is accepted. As for a minor who does not express, he is like a commodity with no possession, so he may testify to ownership in his favor for the person in possession. [3] His statement: 'it is invalid' – it is not accepted because hearsay or seeing in possession does not permit testimony of ownership. The judge is bound to adjudicate ownership based on testimony if it is based on direct sight and observation, or absolute (unspecified) due to possibility of observation, so it is interpreted accordingly. (1) i.e., it is appropriate for the witness to be absolute in performing testimony, not saying it is by hearsay or by the rule of possession, because he would be admitting that he testified without knowledge. And because the judge is only bound by testimony if it is based on eyewitness or absolute (unspecified) due to possibility of observation, so it is interpreted accordingly. See: Fath Bab al-Inayah (3:135).
Source pages p. 544

Commentary
This passage states the Hanafi ruling that if a witness explains to the judge that his testimony is based on hearsay or on the mere fact of possession (yad), the testimony is invalid. The reason is that valid testimony requires certain knowledge ('ilm al-yaqin), which can only come from direct observation (mu'ayanah) or from an unqualified statement that carries that implication. The commentary explains that mere possession (yad) is not sufficient proof of ownership because a free person has possession over himself, so possession alone does not establish ownership. The witness must have certainty that the property belongs to the person in possession. If he qualifies his testimony as based on hearsay or yad, he admits he lacks that certainty, so the testimony is rejected. The passage also touches on the concept of 'ibaarah (expression) and the capacity to express oneself: adults who can express have disposal over themselves, while minors who cannot express are like goods with no possession, so testimony based on their possession may be accepted. This ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah and is the relied-upon position.

Key Terms
التسامع (al-tasamu'): Hearsay; information received from others, not direct observation.
حكم اليد (hukm al-yad): The legal presumption of ownership based on physical possession (yad).
عيان (ayan): Direct visual observation; eyewitness.
يد (yad): Possession; physical control over an object.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a witness says 'I testify that this is his property because I saw it in his hand,' and explains it is based on his possession (yad), what is the ruling according to this passage?
A) The testimony is valid because possession is a strong indication.
B) The testimony is invalid because it is based on hearsay.
C) The testimony is invalid because it is based on mere possession without certainty.
D) The testimony is accepted if the judge believes the witness.

Key Takeaway
A witness who admits his testimony is based on hearsay or mere possession invalidates it; testimony requires certain knowledge from direct observation.

Testimony of Witnessing Burial or Funeral Prayer is Accepted as Eyewitness
Ruling on testimony via burial/funeral attendance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that witnessing burial or funeral prayer is accepted as eyewitness testimony of death and explain the legal reasoning based on the passage.

Core Idea
Attending burial or funeral prayer is legally equivalent to eyewitnessing death.

This lesson examines a specific rule in Hanafi testimony law: a person who testifies to having attended someone's burial or funeral prayer may validly testify to that person's death. The passage explains why such testimony is treated as direct eyewitness, not hearsay, and distinguishes it from other forms of evidence like mere possession or hearsay.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State the ruling: If someone testifies that he witnessed Zaid's burial or prayed over his body, his testimony is accepted as evidence of death, even if he explains that it is based on his attendance.
This is a specific exemption to the general rule that testimony must be based on direct observation.
2
Understand the rationale
The passage explains that death can generally be witnessed directly by only one or two people. Therefore, attending the funeral or burial is considered equivalent to witnessing death itself (كحضور الدفن أو الصلاة بمنزلة المعاينة).
The rationale is that deception is unlikely in such public events.
3
Contrast with hearsay and possession
The passage contrasts this rule with hearsay (تسامع) and mere possession (يد). Hearsay does not increase the witness's knowledge, so it cannot form the basis of testimony. Similarly, seeing property in someone's hand does not alone confer knowledge of ownership.
This is why the burial/funeral exception is significant.
4
Apply the principle to judicial practice
Note that the judge is not bound to accept such testimony if the witness qualifies it as merely based on attendance? Actually, the passage says it is accepted even if the witness interprets it. The judge may still require additional evidence for his own conviction, but the testimony is valid.
This distinction is important in court procedure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومَن شَهِدَ أَنَّهُ شَهِدَ دَفَنَ زيد ، أو صلى عليه، قبلت، وإن فسر وهو عيان
أقول : هذا يؤكد قول أبي يوسف الله : أن بمجرَّدِ اليدِ لا تَحِلُّ الشَّهادة، بل يُشْتَرَطُ أن يقع في قلبه أنه ملكه ، فإنَّه قد قيل : إنَّ قولَ أبي يوسف له تفسير لإطلاق قول محمد في روايةٍ ؛ وذلك لأنَّ مجرَّدَ اليد لو كان سببا لما أبطل إظهار السبب الشَّهادة، فإذا بين أنَّه يشهد بمجرد اليد بطلت شهادته.
ومَن شَهِدَ أَنَّهُ شَهِدَ دَفْنَ زيد، أو صلّى عليه ، قيلت ، وإن فسر وهو عيان) ؛ لأن معاينة الموت لا يكون إلا من واحد أو اثنين، فحضور الدفن، أو الصَّلاةِ بمنزلة المعاينة، ولا يجري في مثل ذلك التلبيس عادة.
أما إذا كانت عن تسامع أو رؤية في يده فلا تزيده علماً، فلا يجوز له أن يحكم بها، ألا ترى أنه لا يجوز له أن يحكم بسماع نفسه ولو تواتر عنده ، ولا برؤية نفسه في يدِ إنسان، فأولى أن لا يجيز بسماع غيره أو برؤية غيره. كذا في «التبيين»
[ 1 ] قوله : يشترط أن يقع في قلبه...الخ ؛ قيل : لو كان ذلك كافياً في الشهادة لقبلها القاضي إذا قيدها الشاهدُ بما استفادَ العلم به من معاينة اليد، وليس كذلك. أجيب : بأنا جعلنا العيان مجوزاً للشاهد، بأن يقدم على الشاهد وذلك ثابت لما قلنا، وأما أنه يلزم القاضي العمل به فلا يلزمه ؛ ولهذا قلنا : إنَّ الرَّجل إذا كانت في يده دار يتصرف فيها تصرُّف المالك، وبيعت دار بجنبها ، فلذي اليد أن يأخذها بالشفعة، والقاضي لا يقضي له عند إنكار المشتري أن تكون الدَّارُ ملكَ الشفيع ؛ لأنَّ العيان ليس سبباً للوجوب. كذا في «العناية»(٢). [٢] قوله : أنه ملكه ؛ فإن وقع في قلبه أنه ملك غيره لا يحل له أن يشهد بالملك لذي اليد ؛ لأنَّ الأصل اعتبار علم اليقين ؛ لجواز الشهادة ، فإذا فات فات شرط الجواز.
[٣] قوله : قبلت شهادته بالاتفاق ؛ وهو أي حضور دفن زيد أو صلاته عليه. عيان ؛ للموت حكماً، حتى لو فسر للقاضي ، قيل : لأنه لم يشهد إلا بما علم توجب قبولها ؛ لدخوله تحت قوله : إلَّا مَن شَهِدَ بِالْحَقِّ وَهُمْ يَعْلَمُونَ ) (۳) ، وقوله وَمَا شَهِدْنَا إِلَّا بِمَا عَلِمْنَا )
(١) تبيين الحقائق» (٤ : ٢١٧). (۲) «العناية» (٦) : ٤٧٠). (٣) الزخرف : ٨٦. (٤) يوسف : ٨١.
Whoever testifies that he witnessed Zaid's burial or prayed over him, his testimony is accepted, even if he interprets it as eyewitness. I say: This confirms the statement of Abu Yusuf – may Allah have mercy on him – that mere possession (yad) does not permit testimony; rather, it is required that it falls in his heart that it is his ownership. For it has been said that Abu Yusuf's statement is an interpretation of Muhammad's absolute statement in one narration. That is because if mere possession were a cause, then revealing the cause would not invalidate the testimony. So if he clarifies that he testifies based on mere possession, his testimony is invalid. Whoever testifies that he witnessed Zaid's burial or prayed over him, it is accepted, even if he interprets it as eyewitness, because witnessing death can only be from one or two persons, so attending burial or prayer is equivalent to eyewitness, and deception is not usual in such cases. As for based on hearsay or seeing in possession, that does not increase his knowledge, so he is not allowed to judge by it. Do you not see that he is not allowed to judge by his own hearing even if it is recurrent (tawatur), nor by seeing himself in the possession of another? So it is even more appropriate that he not accept by hearing others or seeing others. This is stated in al-Tabyin. [1] His statement: 'It is required that it falls in his heart...' It is said: if that were sufficient for testimony, the judge would accept it if the witness qualified it by what he gained knowledge from by witnessing possession, but it is not so. Answer: We made eyewitness a authorization for the witness to proceed with testimony, and that is established as we said. However, it does not bind the judge to act upon it. For this reason, we say: If a man owns a house in his possession and acts as an owner, and a neighboring house is sold, the possessor may take pre-emption (shuf'ah), but the judge does not rule in his favor against the buyer's denial that the house is the property of the pre-emptor, because eyewitness is not a cause for obligation. This is in al-Inayah. [2] His statement: 'that it is his ownership' – if it falls in his heart that it belongs to another, he is not allowed to testify for the possessor, because the principle is considering certain knowledge for permissibility of testimony. So if it is lost, the condition of permissibility is lost. [3] His statement: 'his testimony is accepted by agreement' – i.e., attending Zaid's burial or praying over him is considered eyewitness of death by legal ruling, so even if he interprets to the judge, he only testifies what he knows. It obligates its acceptance under His statement: 'Except those who testify to the truth while they know' (43:86) and His statement: 'We did not testify except with what we knew' (12:81).
Source pages p. 545

Commentary
This passage addresses two related issues. First, it confirms Abu Yusuf's position that testimony based on mere possession (yad) requires that the witness have actual certainty that the possession indicates ownership; mere yad without that certainty is insufficient. Second, it states that testimony of attending someone's burial or funeral prayer is accepted as direct eyewitness of death, because these events are inextricably linked to death. The reason is that death can only be witnessed by one or two persons, but attending the funeral is sufficient evidence. The passage differentiates between hearsay and possession: hearsay does not provide knowledge, and mere possession without certainty is not enough. The commentary also discusses that the judge is not bound to act on eyewitness that does not meet the higher standard of certainty required for judicial decisions. The intra-Hanafi dispute is noted: Abu Yusuf requires internal certainty, while Muhammad's statement is more absolute. The relied-upon position is that testimony must be based on certain knowledge, hence the requirement that the witness state that it falls in his heart that it is ownership.

Key Terms
تسامع (tasamu'): Hearsay; information received from others, not direct observation.
عيان (ayan): Direct visual observation; eyewitness.
يد (yad): Possession; physical control over an object.
دفن (dafn): Burial; the act of interring a deceased person.
صلاة الجنازة (salat al-janazah): Funeral prayer; the prayer performed over the deceased.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because burial and funeral prayer are considered direct observation of the death itself.
B) Because the witness can be trusted.
C) Because the judge can accept any testimony.
D) Because hearsay is allowed in such cases.

Key Takeaway
Attending burial or funeral prayer is a valid legal substitute for direct eyewitness of death in Hanafi testimony law.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Is Mere Possession Sufficient for Testimony?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on testimony based on yad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding whether mere possession (yad) is a sufficient basis for testimony, and apply the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.

Core Idea
Mere possession is not enough for testimony; the witness must have actual certainty of ownership.

This lesson delves into a specific intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf discussed in the passage. While the main text states that testimony based on mere possession invalidates the testimony, the commentary reveals a disagreement between Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad about the underlying principle. Understanding this dispute clarifies the requirements for valid testimony in property cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
The issue is whether testimony based solely on seeing property in someone's hand (yad) is sufficient, or whether the witness must have internal certainty (yaqin) that the person is the owner.
This arises in property disputes.
2
State the position of Abu Yusuf
Abu Yusuf holds that mere possession is not sufficient. The witness must have a conviction in his heart that the possessor is the owner. If the witness states that his testimony is based only on seeing the yad, it is invalid.
His position is supported by the matn.
3
State the position of Muhammad (in one narration)
Imam Muhammad is reported to have said in an absolute statement that possession alone is a valid basis for testimony. However, if the witness clarifies that his testimony is based on possession, then it becomes invalid.
This is a more permissive position.
4
Understand the reasoning
The commentary argues that if mere possession were a valid cause, then revealing that cause (saying 'I testify because I saw it in his hand') would not invalidate the testimony. But the matn says it does invalidate it, proving that possession alone is not sufficient without certainty.
This is a talil (rational argument).
5
Apply the tarjih and mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is Abu Yusuf's view: testimony must be based on certain knowledge (ilm al-yaqin). Mere possession without that certainty is insufficient.
This is consistent with the general principle that testimony requires certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومَن شَهِدَ أَنَّهُ شَهِدَ دَفَنَ زيد ، أو صلى عليه، قبلت، وإن فسر وهو عيان
أقول : هذا يؤكد قول أبي يوسف الله : أن بمجرَّدِ اليدِ لا تَحِلُّ الشَّهادة، بل يُشْتَرَطُ أن يقع في قلبه أنه ملكه ، فإنَّه قد قيل : إنَّ قولَ أبي يوسف له تفسير لإطلاق قول محمد في روايةٍ ؛ وذلك لأنَّ مجرَّدَ اليد لو كان سببا لما أبطل إظهار السبب الشَّهادة، فإذا بين أنَّه يشهد بمجرد اليد بطلت شهادته.
ومَن شَهِدَ أَنَّهُ شَهِدَ دَفْنَ زيد، أو صلّى عليه ، قيلت ، وإن فسر وهو عيان) ؛ لأن معاينة الموت لا يكون إلا من واحد أو اثنين، فحضور الدفن، أو الصَّلاةِ بمنزلة المعاينة، ولا يجري في مثل ذلك التلبيس عادة.
أما إذا كانت عن تسامع أو رؤية في يده فلا تزيده علماً، فلا يجوز له أن يحكم بها، ألا ترى أنه لا يجوز له أن يحكم بسماع نفسه ولو تواتر عنده ، ولا برؤية نفسه في يدِ إنسان، فأولى أن لا يجيز بسماع غيره أو برؤية غيره. كذا في «التبيين»
[ 1 ] قوله : يشترط أن يقع في قلبه...الخ ؛ قيل : لو كان ذلك كافياً في الشهادة لقبلها القاضي إذا قيدها الشاهدُ بما استفادَ العلم به من معاينة اليد، وليس كذلك. أجيب : بأنا جعلنا العيان مجوزاً للشاهد، بأن يقدم على الشاهد وذلك ثابت لما قلنا، وأما أنه يلزم القاضي العمل به فلا يلزمه ؛ ولهذا قلنا : إنَّ الرَّجل إذا كانت في يده دار يتصرف فيها تصرُّف المالك، وبيعت دار بجنبها ، فلذي اليد أن يأخذها بالشفعة، والقاضي لا يقضي له عند إنكار المشتري أن تكون الدَّارُ ملكَ الشفيع ؛ لأنَّ العيان ليس سبباً للوجوب. كذا في «العناية»(٢). [٢] قوله : أنه ملكه ؛ فإن وقع في قلبه أنه ملك غيره لا يحل له أن يشهد بالملك لذي اليد ؛ لأنَّ الأصل اعتبار علم اليقين ؛ لجواز الشهادة ، فإذا فات فات شرط الجواز.
[٣] قوله : قبلت شهادته بالاتفاق ؛ وهو أي حضور دفن زيد أو صلاته عليه. عيان ؛ للموت حكماً، حتى لو فسر للقاضي ، قيل : لأنه لم يشهد إلا بما علم توجب قبولها ؛ لدخوله تحت قوله : إلَّا مَن شَهِدَ بِالْحَقِّ وَهُمْ يَعْلَمُونَ ) (۳) ، وقوله وَمَا شَهِدْنَا إِلَّا بِمَا عَلِمْنَا )
(١) تبيين الحقائق» (٤ : ٢١٧). (۲) «العناية» (٦) : ٤٧٠). (٣) الزخرف : ٨٦. (٤) يوسف : ٨١.
Whoever testifies that he witnessed Zaid's burial or prayed over him, his testimony is accepted, even if he interprets it as eyewitness. I say: This confirms the statement of Abu Yusuf – may Allah have mercy on him – that mere possession (yad) does not permit testimony; rather, it is required that it falls in his heart that it is his ownership. For it has been said that Abu Yusuf's statement is an interpretation of Muhammad's absolute statement in one narration. That is because if mere possession were a cause, then revealing the cause would not invalidate the testimony. So if he clarifies that he testifies based on mere possession, his testimony is invalid. Whoever testifies that he witnessed Zaid's burial or prayed over him, it is accepted, even if he interprets it as eyewitness, because witnessing death can only be from one or two persons, so attending burial or prayer is equivalent to eyewitness, and deception is not usual in such cases. As for based on hearsay or seeing in possession, that does not increase his knowledge, so he is not allowed to judge by it. Do you not see that he is not allowed to judge by his own hearing even if it is recurrent (tawatur), nor by seeing himself in the possession of another? So it is even more appropriate that he not accept by hearing others or seeing others. This is stated in al-Tabyin. [1] His statement: 'It is required that it falls in his heart...' It is said: if that were sufficient for testimony, the judge would accept it if the witness qualified it by what he gained knowledge from by witnessing possession, but it is not so. Answer: We made eyewitness a authorization for the witness to proceed with testimony, and that is established as we said. However, it does not bind the judge to act upon it. For this reason, we say: If a man owns a house in his possession and acts as an owner, and a neighboring house is sold, the possessor may take pre-emption (shuf'ah), but the judge does not rule in his favor against the buyer's denial that the house is the property of the pre-emptor, because eyewitness is not a cause for obligation. This is in al-Inayah. [2] His statement: 'that it is his ownership' – if it falls in his heart that it belongs to another, he is not allowed to testify for the possessor, because the principle is considering certain knowledge for permissibility of testimony. So if it is lost, the condition of permissibility is lost. [3] His statement: 'his testimony is accepted by agreement' – i.e., attending Zaid's burial or praying over him is considered eyewitness of death by legal ruling, so even if he interprets to the judge, he only testifies what he knows. It obligates its acceptance under His statement: 'Except those who testify to the truth while they know' (43:86) and His statement: 'We did not testify except with what we knew' (12:81).
Source pages p. 545

Commentary
This passage addresses two related issues. First, it confirms Abu Yusuf's position that testimony based on mere possession (yad) requires that the witness have actual certainty that the possession indicates ownership; mere yad without that certainty is insufficient. Second, it states that testimony of attending someone's burial or funeral prayer is accepted as direct eyewitness of death, because these events are inextricably linked to death. The reason is that death can only be witnessed by one or two persons, but attending the funeral is sufficient evidence. The passage differentiates between hearsay and possession: hearsay does not provide knowledge, and mere possession without certainty is not enough. The commentary also discusses that the judge is not bound to act on eyewitness that does not meet the higher standard of certainty required for judicial decisions. The intra-Hanafi dispute is noted: Abu Yusuf requires internal certainty, while Muhammad's statement is more absolute. The relied-upon position is that testimony must be based on certain knowledge, hence the requirement that the witness state that it falls in his heart that it is ownership.

Key Terms
تسامع (tasamu'): Hearsay
عيان (ayan): Direct visual observation
يد (yad): Possession; physical control over an object.
يقين (yaqin): Certainty; firm conviction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi (relied-upon position) in the Hanafi school, what is required for valid testimony based on possession?
A) The witness must have seen the property in the person's hand.
B) The witness must have certain knowledge (yaqin) that the person is the owner.
C) The witness must be a relative of the possessor.
D) The witness must have a written document.

Key Takeaway
Mere possession (yad) is not a sufficient basis for testimony; the witness must have certain knowledge (yaqin) of ownership.

Testimony on Death: Certain Knowledge Required
Ruling on acceptance of testimony based on burial/funeral prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which testimony regarding death is accepted in Hanafi law, distinguish between direct observation and hearsay, and apply the Quranic requirement of knowledge to testimony.

Core Idea
Testimony requires certain knowledge; witnessing burial or funeral prayer is legally equivalent to witnessing death itself.

In this passage, we examine a cornerstone of Hanafi evidence law: the requirement that testimony be based on certain knowledge. The text clarifies when a witness's report of a person's death is admissible, and it draws an important distinction between direct observation (ayan) and mere hearsay or possession. The principles stated here are derived from Quranic verses that anchor testimony in knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is testifying that one witnessed the burial or funeral prayer of Zaid. The ruling: such testimony is accepted (مقبولة).
2
Analyze the reasoning
Why is it accepted? Because witnessing death directly is often impossible for multiple people, so attending burial/prayer is equivalent to direct observation, and deception is unlikely.
3
Contrast with rejected bases
Testimony based on hearsay or mere possession is rejected because it does not provide certain knowledge.
The text uses a logical analogy: if one cannot rule by their own hearing or seeing in some contexts, then a fortiori they cannot rely on others' hearing or seeing.
4
Extract the Quranic dalil
The passage cites two verses: 'إِلَّا مَن شَهِدَ بِالْحَقِّ وَهُمْ يَعْلَمُونَ' and 'وَمَا شَهِدْنَا إِلَّا بِمَا عَلِمْنَا' to establish that testimony must be based on knowledge.
These verses are used by Hanafis to require certain knowledge (yaqeen) for testimony.
5
Consider the intra-Hanafi dispute
Regarding testimony based on possession, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad require internal certainty, while Abu Hanifa reportedly allowed it in some transmissions. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) follows the Sahibayn.
The commentator affirms Abu Yusuf's view as stronger.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومَن شَهِدَ أَنَّهُ شَهِدَ دَفَنَ زيد، أو صلى عليه، قُبِلَت، وإن فَسَّرَ وهو عيان؛ لأن معاينة الموت لا يكون إلا من واحد أو اثنين، فحضور الدفن أو الصلاة بمنزلة المعاينة، ولا يجري في مثل ذلك التلبيس عادة. أما إذا كانت عن تسامع أو رؤية في يده فلا تزيده علماً، فلا يجوز له أن يحكم بها. ألا ترى أنه لا يجوز له أن يحكم بسماع نفسه ولو تواتر عنده، ولا برؤية نفسه في يد إنسان، فأولى أن لا يجيز بسماع غيره أو برؤية غيره. كذا في «التبيين». قوله: يشترط أن يقع في قلبه أنه ملكه؛ فإن وقع في قلبه أنه ملك غيره لا يحل له أن يشهد بالملك لذي اليد؛ لأن الأصل اعتبار علم اليقين لجواز الشهادة، فإذا فات فات شرط الجواز. وقبلت شهادته بالاتفاق، وهو أي حضور دفن زيد أو صلاته عليه عيان للموت حكماً، حتى لو فسر للقاضي؛ لأنه لم يشهد إلا بما علم، توجب قبولها؛ لدخوله تحت قوله: {إِلَّا مَن شَهِدَ بِالْحَقِّ وَهُمْ يَعْلَمُونَ} [الزخرف: 86]، وقوله: {وَمَا شَهِدْنَا إِلَّا بِمَا عَلِمْنَا} [يوسف: 81].
And whoever testifies that he witnessed the burial of Zaid or that he prayed over him, it is accepted, even if he explains (that it is based on) direct observation; because witnessing death is not possible except for one or two, so attending the burial or the prayer is equivalent to direct observation, and deception does not usually occur in such matters. As for (testimony) based on hearsay or seeing (an item) in his hand, it does not increase his knowledge, so it is not permissible for him to rule by it. Do you not see that it is not permissible for him to rule by his own hearing even if it is mutawatir for him, nor by his own seeing of something in a person's hand; so it is more appropriate that he not permit (testimony) based on the hearing of others or the seeing of others. This is as in 'al-Tabyin'. His statement: 'It is required that it occur in his heart that it is his property'; if it occurs in his heart that it is the property of another, it is not lawful for him to testify to ownership for the possessor; because the principle is consideration of certain knowledge for the permissibility of testimony; if it is missing, the condition of permissibility is missing. And his testimony is accepted by agreement, and that is, attending the burial of Zaid or praying over him is deemed direct observation of death, even if he explains to the judge; because he only testifies to what he knows, and it necessitates its acceptance because it falls under His saying: 'Except those who bear witness to the truth while they know' [al-Zukhruf 43:86], and His saying: 'And we did not testify except to what we knew' [Yusuf 12:81].
Source pages p. 545

Commentary
This passage is from the book of testimony, explaining when testimony regarding death is accepted. The author states a clear ruling: if a person testifies that he witnessed the burial of a deceased person or that he prayed the funeral prayer over him, such testimony is accepted in court. Even if the witness explains that his knowledge is based on direct observation (عيان) of the burial or prayer, it does not invalidate the testimony. The reasoning is that only one or two persons can directly witness the moment of death; thus, attending the burial or funeral prayer serves as strong circumstantial evidence of death, and deception is unlikely in such public acts. The passage then contrasts this with testimony based on hearsay (تسامع) or mere observation of an item in someone's hand. Such testimony does not provide certain knowledge and is therefore impermissible. The author illustrates with the principle that a person cannot rule by his own hearing or seeing in certain contexts, so a fortiori he cannot rely on others' hearing or seeing. The commentary cites two Quranic verses: 'Except those who bear witness to the truth while they know' and 'We did not testify except to what we knew.' These establish that testimony must be based on certain knowledge. The passage also touches on an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding testimony based on possession (اليد). Abu Yusuf holds that mere possession is not sufficient; the witness must have certainty in his heart that the possessor is the owner. If he doubts, he cannot testify. This is because the foundation of testimony is certain knowledge. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is that testimony based on possession requires such certainty, and the witness must not base his testimony solely on apparent possession.

Key Terms
عيان (ayan): Direct sensory observation; seeing an event with one's own eyes.
تسامع (tasamu'): Hearsay; information received from others, not based on personal observation.
يد (yad): Possession; physical control or custody of an object.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why is testimony based on witnessing burial or funeral prayer accepted even if the witness explains that it is from direct observation?
A) Because the witness has seen the body
B) Because burial or prayer is public and deception is unlikely, and it serves as equivalent to witnessing death
C) Because the Quran specifically mentions burial testimony
D) Because the witness can hear the funeral prayers

Key Takeaway
Testimony must be based on certain knowledge; witnessing burial or funeral prayer provides that certainty and is legally equivalent to witnessing death itself.

Shahadat — Testimony

The Meaning of Acceptance (Qabūl) in Testimony
Explaining the meaning of qabūl and the conditions for accepting testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi distinction between qabūl (obligation to accept) and sihhah (validity), apply the ruling to the case of a fāsiq, and identify exceptions where acceptance is mandatory.

Core Idea
Acceptance (qabūl) means the judge must accept the testimony; non-acceptance does not invalidate a judgment.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'āyah clarifies the crucial distinction between the validity of testimony (sihhah) and the obligation of the judge to accept it (qabūl). It defines the term qabūl linguistically and legally, explains the effect of a judge accepting testimony from a fāsiq, and lists exceptions where testimony must be accepted despite apparent defects. Understanding this distinction is fundamental to Hanafi procedural fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic meaning of qabūl
The passage cites lexicons to show qabūl means 'to believe' or 'to accept'. Note that the legal meaning builds on this.
2
Distinguish qabūl from sihhah
The author explicitly states: 'المراد من يجب قبول شهادته على القاضي، ومن لا يجب لا من يصح قبولها' — the intended meaning is obligation, not validity.
This is the key conceptual distinction.
3
Analyze the ruling on a fāsiq witness
The passage says testimony of a fāsiq is not accepted (i.e., not obligatory), but if the judge accepts it and gives judgment, the judgment is valid and cannot be annulled.
Contrast with other defective witnesses where judgment is invalid.
4
Contrast with other defective witnesses
For a slave, child, wife, parent/child, judgment is not valid if based on their testimony. The difference with the fāsiq is rooted in the nature of the defect.
The fāsiq's defect is in conduct, not in legal capacity.
5
Note the exceptions from Khizanat al-Muftiyyin
The passage lists cases where judgment based on testimony of certain apparently defective witnesses (blind, punished for qadhf after repentance, one spouse for the other, parent for child) is also valid and cannot be annulled.
These exceptions illustrate the principle that enforceability does not always depend on obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقبل الشهادة باب القبول وعدمه بعد الفراغ عن بيان ما تسمع فيه الشهادة وما لا تسمع ، أراد المصنف له أن يشرع في بيان من تسمعُ منه الشهادة، ومن لا تسمع منه ، ووجه تقديم ذلك البيان ظاهر، فإنّه محالُ الشهادة، والمحال شروط له. والشروط تتقدم على المشروط طبعاً ، فالتقدم وضعاً أولى بالنظر إلى التوافق والقبول، بالضم: پیش آیدن دوزیدن با وصباد دلورا من ساقي گرفتن، وبالفتح : يذير فتن، وهو مصدر شاد. كذا «الصراح». والثاني : هو المراد هاهنا ، يقال : قبلتُ العقد أقبله من باب تعب، قبولاً بالفتح ، والضم لغة حكاها ابن الأَعْرَابي، وقبلتُ القولَ: صدقته، وقبلت الهدية : أخذتها. كذا في «المصباح». والمراد من يجب قبول شهادته على القاضي ، ومَن لا يجب لا مَن يصح قبولها ، ومَن لا يصح؛ لأنَّ من جملة ما ذكره من لا تقبل الفاسق، وهو لو قضى بشهادته صح بخلاف العبدِ والصبي والزوجة والولد والأصل. لكن في خزانة المفتيين»: إذا قضى بشهادة الأعمى أو المحدود في القذف إذا تاب، أو بشهادة أحد الزوجين مع آخر لصاحبه، أو بشهادة الوالد لولده أو عكسه نفذ حتى لا يجوز للثاني إبطاله، وإن رأى بطلانه. انتهى. فالمراد من عدم القبول عدم حله. كذا في «البحر» قوله : ويقبلُ الشهادة؛ مطلقاً سواء كان على أهل السنة أو بعضهم على بعض ، أو على الكفرة إذا لم يكن اعتقاد أهل الأهواء مؤدياً إلى الكفر. كما في «الذخيرة» وغيرها، وإنما قبلت شهادتهم ؛ لأنَّ فسقهم من حيث الاعتقاد، وما أوقعه
And testimony is accepted. Chapter of acceptance and non-acceptance. After finishing the explanation of where testimony is heard and where not, the author intended to begin explaining from whom testimony is heard and from whom it is not heard. The reason for placing this explanation before is apparent, for it is the subject of testimony, and the subject has conditions. Conditions naturally precede the conditioned, so precedence in position is even more appropriate considering compatibility and acceptance. With damm: [Persian phrase meaning?] and with fatha: [Persian phrase meaning?] and it is the verbal noun of shadda. Similarly in 'al-Sirah'. And the second meaning is intended here. It is said: qabiltu al-'aqda aqbaluhu from the verb form of ta'iba, qabulan with fatha, and with damm is a language narrated by Ibn al-A'rabi. And qabiltu al-qawla: I believed it, and qabiltu al-hadiyata: I took it. Similarly in al-Misbah. What is intended is the one whose testimony must be accepted by the judge, and the one whose testimony must not be accepted, not the one whose testimony is valid or invalid; because among those mentioned are those whose testimony is not accepted, like the fasiq, and if he gives judgment based on his testimony, it is valid, unlike the slave, the child, the wife, and the son and the father. But in Khizanat al-Muftiyyin: If he gives judgment based on the testimony of a blind person, or a person who has been punished for qadhf after repentance, or based on the testimony of one of the spouses with another concerning his spouse, or the testimony of a parent for his child or vice versa, it is executed, to the extent that it is not permissible for the second [judge] to annul it, even if he sees its invalidity. End. So what is meant by non-acceptance is its lack of validity. Similarly in al-Bahr. He said: And testimony is accepted absolutely, whether it is concerning Ahl al-Sunnah or some of them against some, or against disbelievers, as long as the belief of the people of desires does not lead to kufr. As in al-Dhakhirah and others. And their testimony is accepted because their transgression is in terms of belief, and what has occurred...
Source pages p. 546

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the chapter heading 'باب القبول وعدمه' and the phrase 'ويقبل الشهادة' from the matn. It first defines the term 'قبول' linguistically, citing sources like al-Sirah and al-Misbah. The commentary clarifies that the intended meaning is not the validity of testimony (sihhah) but rather the obligation of the judge to accept it (wujub al-qabul). It then discusses the ruling of a fasiq (transgressor): although his testimony is not accepted (i.e., the judge is not obligated to accept it), if the judge bases a judgment on it, the judgment is valid and cannot be overturned by another judge. This is contrasted with the testimony of a slave, child, wife, or parent/child, where judgment would not be valid. The commentary also notes exceptions from Khizanat al-Muftiyyin and al-Bahr regarding acceptance of testimony from a blind person or one punished for qadhf after tawbah. Finally, it states that testimony is accepted even from Ahl al-Ahwa (people of desires) as long as their beliefs do not amount to kufr, quoting al-Dhakhirah.

Key Terms
شهادة (shahādah): Testimony given in a court of law; the legal act of bearing witness.
قبول (qabūl): The judge's acceptance of testimony, meaning he is obligated to consider it; not the validity of the testimony itself.
فاسق (fāsiq): A person who commits major sins or persists in minor sins; his testimony is not accepted (i.e., not obligatory on the judge), but judgment based on it is enforceable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence if a judge gives judgment based on the testimony of a fasiq?
A) The judgment is void and must be overturned.
B) The judgment is valid and cannot be annulled by another judge.
C) The judgment is valid but can be annulled by appeal.
D) The judgment is suspended until the fasiq repents.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, 'non-acceptance' of a fasiq's testimony means no obligation on the judge, but if accepted, the judgment stands.

Accepting Testimony from People of Innovation (Ahl al-Ahwa')
Ruling on accepting testimony from people of innovation except Khattabiyyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on accepting testimony from people of innovation and identify the exception of the Khattabiyyah, applying the distinction between fisq in belief versus action.

Core Idea
Testimony of a person of innovation is accepted because their sin is in belief, not action – unless their belief reaches kufr.

In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, the Hanafi school clarifies which witnesses are admissible in court. The focus is on those who hold innovative beliefs (ahl al-ahwa') but still belong to ahl al-qibla. The key ruling is that their testimony is accepted unless their innovation amounts to disbelief (kufr), as in the case of the Khattabiyyah sect. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the exceptional case.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is 'accepting testimony' from a witness. The ruling is that it is permissible (mubah) for the judge to accept it, and in fact obligatory (wajib) if the witness meets conditions. But here the focus is on whether testimony from ahl al-ahwa' is acceptable at all.
The passage states 'wa yuqbal al-shahadah' – testimony is accepted absolutely.
2
Locate the dalil
The author provides an analogy (qiyas): the person of innovation is like one who drinks wine or eats meat without tasmiyah while deeming it permissible. Such actions are sinful but do not nullify the person's testimony.
The dalil is qiyas, not a direct text.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The analogy works because the sin is in belief ('aqidah), not in action ('amal). Just as a sinful action (like drinking wine) does not automatically make one a liar or biased, similarly a sinful belief does not unless it reaches kufr.
Contrast with fisq bi al-ta'ati (sin in action) which does affect credibility.
4
Note the exception
The Khattabiyyah are singled out because their belief (deifying Ali and Ja'far) is kufr, and they are also biased in testimony, believing it obligatory to testify for fellow sect members.
Their testimony is rejected on two grounds: kufr and bias.
5
Evaluate the epistemological weight
This is the mufta bihi position in the later Hanafi school (fatawa mutakhkhirin). While some earlier scholars or other schools may reject all innovators' testimony, the Hanafi position distinguishes based on the nature of the sin.
Authority weight: fatawa mutakhkhirin.

I accepted the statement: I believed it. I accepted the gift: I took it. As in al-Misbah. What is intended is he whose testimony must be accepted by the judge, and he whose testimony must not be accepted, not he whose testimony is valid to accept and he whose testimony is not valid, because among what he mentioned is one whose testimony is not accepted, the sinful person, and if he gave judgement by his testimony it is valid, contrary to the slave, child, wife, child and parent. [1] His saying: from the people of innovation; al-ahwa' is plural of hawa, source of 'hawaytuhu': when he loved and desired it, then called the desired object by it, praised or blamed, then dominant in blame. It is said: So-and-so followed his whim when blame is intended. And so-and-so is from the people of innovation: for one who deviates from the way of Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah and is among the people of the qibla. As in al-Mughrib. And they are also called people of innovation (ahl al-bida'); hence it occurs in al-Talwih in 'Rukn al-Sunnah': al-hawa is inclination towards desires and pleasures without the call of the Law, and what is meant by the one of whim is the innovator inclined to one he desires in matters of religion. He was killed and crucified by 'Isa in al-Kanasa, which is a locality in Kufa; because he claimed that 'Ali is the greater deity and Ja'far al-Sadiq the lesser deity. They believe that if one of them claims something against another, it is obligatory to testify for him out of taqiyya. And it is said: they believe in testifying for one who swears that he is truthful, and they say: a Muslim does not swear falsely, so suspicion of falsehood arises in their testimony. And it is said: they consider testifying for their fellow Shia obligatory, so suspicion arises in their testimony, so it is not accepted. [3] His saying: People of the qibla; what is meant by people of the qibla are those who agree on what is among the essentials of religion, such as the temporal origination of the world, the resurrection of bodies, the knowledge of Allah of universals and particulars, and similar important issues.
Source pages p. 547

Commentary
This is a commentary passage from the Hanafi fiqh work 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah 'ala Sharh al-Wiqayah, expanding on the chapter of acceptance and non-acceptance of testimony (shahada). The author discusses the ruling on accepting testimony from people of innovation (ahl al-ahwa') and explains the exception of the Khattabiyyah sect. The passage begins by defining the scope: after clarifying in which cases testimony is heard or not, the author turns to the conditions of the witness. The term 'qabul' (acceptance) is linguistically analyzed: it means both 'coming forward' and 'accepting', and the latter is intended here. The intended meaning is not whether testimony is valid in itself, but whether the judge is obligated to accept it. For example, the testimony of a fasiq (sinner) is technically valid if the judge acts on it, but it is not obligatory for him to accept it, unlike the testimony of a slave, child, spouse, or parent/child which is invalid from the outset. However, the Khizanat al-Muftiyyin notes that in certain cases even testimony that is defective (like that of a blind man or a hadd-punished for qadhf after repentance) if the judge passes judgement based on it, it is enforceable. Then the key ruling: testimony is accepted absolutely from all Muslims, whether for or against Ahl al-Sunnah or others, including from one Muslim against another Muslim or even against a non-Muslim, provided the person's belief does not lead to outright kufr (disbelief). The logic is that their transgression is in belief (i'tiqad) not in action, so they are like one who drinks wine or eats meat without tasmiyah while deeming it permissible — such actions are sins but do not render their testimony categorically rejected like those whose actions are sinful (fisq bi al-ta'ati). The exception is the Khattabiyyah: a sect that deified 'Ali and Ja'far, thus their testimony is not accepted because their belief leads to kufr. The passage defines ahl al-ahwa' as those who deviate from the Sunni path but still pray towards the qibla. Six origins of heresy are listed: Jabr (compulsion), Qadr (free will), Rafd (rejection of the first three caliphs), Khuruj (rebellion against ruler), Shubhah (inducing doubts), and Ta'til (denial of divine attributes). The Khattabiyyah are specifically rejected because they hold the belief that it is obligatory to testify on behalf of fellow sect members in court, leading to bias and suspicion of falsehood. The passage concludes by defining 'ahl al-qibla' as those who affirm the fundamentals of Islamic creed, which the Khattabiyyah do not.

Key Terms
أهل الأهواء (ahl al-ahwa'): People of innovation (bid'a) who deviate from the Sunni path but still pray towards the qibla; their sin is in belief.
الخطابية (al-Khattabiyyah): A sect that deified 'Ali and Ja'far; their testimony is rejected because their belief is kufr and they are biased in testimony.
أهل القبلة (ahl al-qibla): Those who agree on essential Islamic beliefs such as the temporal origin of the world, resurrection, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school accept the testimony of a person of innovation?
A) Because their sin is in action, not belief.
B) Because their sin is in belief, not action, and belief-related sins do not render testimony invalid unless they lead to kufr.
C) Because all Muslims' testimony is accepted unconditionally.
D) Because they are considered trustworthy despite their innovation.

Key Takeaway
Testimony from a person of innovation is accepted as long as their belief does not amount to kufr, except for the Khattabiyyah who are rejected due to extreme beliefs and bias.

Shahadat — Testimony
intermediate
8 min
Definition of Ahl al-Ahwa' and Their Sects
Defining ahl al-ahwa' and listing deviant sects whose testimony is rejected.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define ahl al-ahwa', list the five main sects, and explain why their testimony is rejected in Hanafi jurisprudence.

Core Idea
Ahl al-Ahwa' are Muslims with deviant beliefs; their testimony is rejected due to corruption of integrity or suspicion of bias.

In Islamic court, a witness must be upright ('adl). The Hanafi school defines deviant beliefs that compromise this uprightness. This lesson defines 'ahl al-ahwa'' and examines the specific sects whose testimony is rejected, including the exceptional case of the Khattabiyyah.

Guided Path
1
Understand the term 'ahl al-ahwa''
Trace the linguistic root of 'hawa' (desire) and how it came to denote blameworthy deviation from the Sunnah. Note that these individuals remain Muslims (people of the Qibla) but hold beliefs contrary to Ahl al-Sunnah.
2
Learn the six categories of deviance
The text lists six origins: Jabr (compulsion), Qadar (power), Rafd (rejection of Caliphs), Khuruj (secession), Shubha (ambiguity), and Ta'til (denial of attributes). These map to the five named sects plus an unelaborated category.
Shubha (ambiguity) is not detailed; focus on the five named sects.
3
Examine each sect's belief and refutation
For each sect, identify the core belief and the Hanafi refutation: Jabariyyah nullify reward/punishment, Qadariyyah contradict the Quran, etc. Understand why these beliefs make the witness unreliable.
Focus on the legal consequence for testimony, not the theological debate.
4
Analyze the Khattabiyyah exception
The Khattabiyyah are mentioned separately because their rejection is based on tuhma (suspicion of bias) from their doctrine of obligatory testimony for coreligionists, not general fasiq.
This exception shows that the ruling is nuanced and context-specific.

قوله: أهل القبلة؛ المراد بأهل القبلة الذي اتفقوا على ما هو من ضرورات الدين، كحدوث العالم، وحشر الأجساد، وعلم الله تعالى بالكليات والجزئيات، وما أشبه ذلك من المسائل المهمات. فمن واظب طول عمره على الطاعات والعبادات مع اعتقاد قدم العالم أو نفي الحشر أو نفي علمه سبحانه بالجزئيات لا يكون من أهل القبلة. كما صرح به. والتفصيل في «شرح الفقه الأكبر» لعلي القاري رحمه الله الباري.
قوله: وهم الجبرية؛ وهم الذين قالوا: إن العبد مجبر لا قدرة له أصلاً لا خالقة ولا كاسبة، ويرد عليهم بطلان الثواب والعقاب.
القدرية: وهم الذين قالوا: إن للعبد قدرة خالقة لأفعاله، ويرد عليهم قوله تعالى: {وَاللهُ خَلَقَكُمْ وَمَا تَعْمَلُونَ}.
والروافض: وهم الذين رفضوا أكثر الصحابة، وأنكروا إمامة الشيخين، والمسح على الخفين، وسبوا معاوية له وأحزابه.
والخوارج: وهم الذين خرجوا عن الطريقة السوية، وحاربوا مع علي رضي الله عنه، وشتموا أصهار رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم.

From the people of desires except the Khattabiyyah. The people of desires are the people of the Qibla whose belief is not the belief of the people of the Sunnah and the community. They are the Jabarites, the Qadarites, the Rawafid, the Khawarij, and the Mu'attilah.
[His statement: "from the people of desires"; al-ahwa' is the plural of hawa, the masdar of "hawituhu": when you love and desire it. Then it is used to name the desired and longed for, whether praiseworthy or blameworthy. Then it came to predominate in the blameworthy sense. It is said: "So-and-so follows his desire" when one intends to blame him, and "So-and-so is from the people of desires" for one who deviates from the way of the people of the Sunnah and the community, while being from the people of the Qibla. This is mentioned in al-Mughrib. The people of desires are also called the people of innovations; hence it appears in al-Talwih in the "Pillar of the Sunnah": "Desire is inclination towards pleasures and delights without a call from the Shari'a. The intended meaning of the person of desire is the innovator who inclines towards whom he desires in matters of religion." The origins of the people of desires are six: Jabr (compulsion), Qadar (predestination), Rafd (rejection), Khuruj (secession), Shubha (ambiguity), and Ta'til (negation).
His statement: "the Khattabiyyah"; they are a group attributed to Abu al-Khattab Muhammad bin Wahb al-Ajda', or said: Muhammad bin Abi Zainab al-Asadi al-Ajda'. He was a man in Kufa, who fought Isa bin Musa bin Ali bin Abdullah bin Abbas, and claimed authority for Ja'far, but Ja'far disassociated from him and cursed him. He and his companions were killed, killed and crucified by Isa in al-Kunasa, which is a locality in Kufa; because he claimed that 'Ali is the greater deity and Ja'far al-Sadiq is the lesser deity. They believe that whoever among them claims something against another must testify for him out of dissimulation. Or it is said: they believe in testifying for one who swears that he is right, and they say: a Muslim does not swear falsely, so the suspicion of falsehood becomes possible in their testimony. Or it is said: they consider testimony for their followers obligatory, so suspicion lurks in their testimony, hence it is not accepted.
His statement: "people of the Qibla"; what is meant by people of the Qibla are those who agree on matters of religious necessities, such as the temporal creation of the world, the resurrection of bodies, and the knowledge of Allah the Exalted of universals and particulars, and similar important issues. So whoever persists his entire life in acts of obedience and worship while believing in the eternity of the world, or denying resurrection, or denying His knowledge of particulars, is not from the people of the Qibla. As explicitly stated. The detail is in "Sharh al-Fiqh al-Akbar" by 'Ali al-Qari, may Allah have mercy on him.
His statement: "and they are the Jabarites"; they are those who said: the servant is compelled, with no power at all, neither creative nor acquisitive. The nullification of reward and punishment refutes them.
The Qadarites: they are those who said: the servant has a creative power for his actions, and the verse: {And Allah created you and what you do} refutes them.
And the Rawafid: they are those who rejected most of the Companions, denied the caliphate of the two Shaykhs (Abu Bakr and 'Umar), denied wiping over the socks, and cursed Mu'awiyah and his party.
And the Khawarij: they are those who deviated from the correct path, fought against 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him), and insulted the in-laws of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him).
And the Mu'attilah: they are those who said: Allah the Exalted is devoid (of attributes), as the philosophers said: He is an emanation.
Source pages p. 548

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the ruling that the testimony of ahl al-ahwa' (people of desires) is not accepted, with the exception of the Khattabiyyah sect. The commentator defines ahl al-ahwa' as Muslims (people of the Qibla) whose beliefs deviate from the Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah. The term "hawa" originally means love and desire, but came to refer to blameworthy desires that lead one away from the Sunnah. The people of desires are also called people of innovation (bid'ah). The commentator lists six fundamental categories of deviance: Jabr (compulsion in actions), Qadar (capacity to create actions), Rafd (rejection of the first two caliphs), Khuruj (secession/shunning the community), Shubha (skepticism/ambiguity), and Ta'til (denial of divine attributes).

The commentary then details each sect: the Khattabiyyah are an extremist group who claimed divinity for 'Ali and Ja'far al-Sadiq; their testimony is rejected due to their doctrine of permissible lying (taqiyya) and biased testimony. The Jabarites deny human agency entirely, which would negate the basis for reward and punishment. The Qadarites assert humans create their own actions, contradicting the Qur'anic verse that Allah is the Creator of all. The Rawafid (a term for extreme Shi'a) reject the legitimacy of Abu Bakr and 'Umar, reject wiping over socks, and curse Mu'awiyah. The Khawarij seceded from the community and fought against 'Ali, insulting his family. The Mu'attilah are those who deny God's attributes, following philosophical doctrines of emanation rather than divine creation.

Notably, the commentator emphasizes that even if a person performs all acts of worship perfectly, if they hold a foundational belief that contradicts the essentials of religion (like the eternity of the world or denial of resurrection), they are not considered Muslims (people of the Qibla). This ruling affects the acceptance of their testimony in Islamic courts.

Key Terms
أهل الأهواء (ahl al-ahwa'): Muslims (people of the Qibla) whose beliefs deviate from the Sunni creed, particularly in matters of qadar, attributes, and the Companions.
الخطابية (al-Khattabiyyah): A sect attributing divinity to 'Ali and Ja'far al-Sadiq; they permit false testimony under dissimulation (taqiyya).
الجبرية (al-Jabariyyah): Those who deny any human power or agency, making God the sole agent of all actions.
القدرية (al-Qadariyyah): Those who affirm that humans have a creative power (qudra khalqiyya) for their actions, distinct from God's power.
الروافض (al-Rawafid): Extreme Shi'a who reject the caliphate of Abu Bakr and 'Umar, and revile many Companions.
الخوارج (al-Khawarij): Seceders who rebelled against 'Ali and considered major sins as kufr, thus excommunicating many Muslims.
المعطلة (al-Mu'attilah): Those who negate God's attributes, often following philosophical doctrines of emanation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position, why is the testimony of the Khattabiyyah rejected?
A) Because they are not Muslims
B) Because they believe it is obligatory to testify for their co-religionists, creating suspicion of bias
C) Because they are polytheists
D) Because they reject the Qibla

Key Takeaway
The testimony of ahl al-ahwa' is rejected to preserve judicial integrity; the Khattabiyyah are singled out because their rejection is based on tuhma, not inherent fasiq.
